2852	"Mr. Sherlock Holmes"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"      Mr. Sherlock Holmes, who was usually very late in the mornings,"
2852	"      save upon those not infrequent occasions when he was up all"
2852	"      night, was seated at the breakfast table. I stood upon the"
2852	"      hearth-rug and picked up the stick which our visitor had left"
2852	"      behind him the night before. It was a fine, thick piece of wood,"
2852	"      bulbous-headed, of the sort which is known as a “Penang lawyer.”"
2852	"      Just under the head was a broad silver band nearly an inch"
2852	"      across. “To James Mortimer, M.R.C.S., from his friends of the"
2852	"      C.C.H.,” was engraved upon it, with the date “1884.” It was just"
2852	"      such a stick as the old-fashioned family practitioner used to"
2852	"      carry—dignified, solid, and reassuring."
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, Watson, what do you make of it?”"
2852	""
2852	"      Holmes was sitting with his back to me, and I had given him no"
2852	"      sign of my occupation."
2852	""
2852	"      “How did you know what I was doing? I believe you have eyes in"
2852	"      the back of your head.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I have, at least, a well-polished, silver-plated coffee-pot in"
2852	"      front of me,” said he. “But, tell me, Watson, what do you make of"
2852	"      our visitor’s stick? Since we have been so unfortunate as to miss"
2852	"      him and have no notion of his errand, this accidental souvenir"
2852	"      becomes of importance. Let me hear you reconstruct the man by an"
2852	"      examination of it.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I think,” said I, following as far as I could the methods of my"
2852	"      companion, “that Dr. Mortimer is a successful, elderly medical"
2852	"      man, well-esteemed since those who know him give him this mark of"
2852	"      their appreciation.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Good!” said Holmes. “Excellent!”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I think also that the probability is in favour of his being a"
2852	"      country practitioner who does a great deal of his visiting on"
2852	"      foot.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Why so?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Because this stick, though originally a very handsome one has"
2852	"      been so knocked about that I can hardly imagine a town"
2852	"      practitioner carrying it. The thick-iron ferrule is worn down, so"
2852	"      it is evident that he has done a great amount of walking with"
2852	"      it.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Perfectly sound!” said Holmes."
2852	""
2852	"      “And then again, there is the ‘friends of the C.C.H.’ I should"
2852	"      guess that to be the Something Hunt, the local hunt to whose"
2852	"      members he has possibly given some surgical assistance, and which"
2852	"      has made him a small presentation in return.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Really, Watson, you excel yourself,” said Holmes, pushing back"
2852	"      his chair and lighting a cigarette. “I am bound to say that in"
2852	"      all the accounts which you have been so good as to give of my own"
2852	"      small achievements you have habitually underrated your own"
2852	"      abilities. It may be that you are not yourself luminous, but you"
2852	"      are a conductor of light. Some people without possessing genius"
2852	"      have a remarkable power of stimulating it. I confess, my dear"
2852	"      fellow, that I am very much in your debt.”"
2852	""
2852	"      He had never said as much before, and I must admit that his words"
2852	"      gave me keen pleasure, for I had often been piqued by his"
2852	"      indifference to my admiration and to the attempts which I had"
2852	"      made to give publicity to his methods. I was proud, too, to think"
2852	"      that I had so far mastered his system as to apply it in a way"
2852	"      which earned his approval. He now took the stick from my hands"
2852	"      and examined it for a few minutes with his naked eyes. Then with"
2852	"      an expression of interest he laid down his cigarette, and"
2852	"      carrying the cane to the window, he looked over it again with a"
2852	"      convex lens."
2852	""
2852	"      “Interesting, though elementary,” said he as he returned to his"
2852	"      favourite corner of the settee. “There are certainly one or two"
2852	"      indications upon the stick. It gives us the basis for several"
2852	"      deductions.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Has anything escaped me?” I asked with some self-importance. “I"
2852	"      trust that there is nothing of consequence which I have"
2852	"      overlooked?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I am afraid, my dear Watson, that most of your conclusions were"
2852	"      erroneous. When I said that you stimulated me I meant, to be"
2852	"      frank, that in noting your fallacies I was occasionally guided"
2852	"      towards the truth. Not that you are entirely wrong in this"
2852	"      instance. The man is certainly a country practitioner. And he"
2852	"      walks a good deal.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Then I was right.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “To that extent.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But that was all.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, no, my dear Watson, not all—by no means all. I would"
2852	"      suggest, for example, that a presentation to a doctor is more"
2852	"      likely to come from a hospital than from a hunt, and that when"
2852	"      the initials ‘C.C.’ are placed before that hospital the words"
2852	"      ‘Charing Cross’ very naturally suggest themselves.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “You may be right.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “The probability lies in that direction. And if we take this as a"
2852	"      working hypothesis we have a fresh basis from which to start our"
2852	"      construction of this unknown visitor.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, then, supposing that ‘C.C.H.’ does stand for ‘Charing"
2852	"      Cross Hospital,’ what further inferences may we draw?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Do none suggest themselves? You know my methods. Apply them!”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I can only think of the obvious conclusion that the man has"
2852	"      practised in town before going to the country.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I think that we might venture a little farther than this. Look"
2852	"      at it in this light. On what occasion would it be most probable"
2852	"      that such a presentation would be made? When would his friends"
2852	"      unite to give him a pledge of their good will? Obviously at the"
2852	"      moment when Dr. Mortimer withdrew from the service of the"
2852	"      hospital in order to start a practice for himself. We know there"
2852	"      has been a presentation. We believe there has been a change from"
2852	"      a town hospital to a country practice. Is it, then, stretching"
2852	"      our inference too far to say that the presentation was on the"
2852	"      occasion of the change?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “It certainly seems probable.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Now, you will observe that he could not have been on the _staff_"
2852	"      of the hospital, since only a man well-established in a London"
2852	"      practice could hold such a position, and such a one would not"
2852	"      drift into the country. What was he, then? If he was in the"
2852	"      hospital and yet not on the staff he could only have been a"
2852	"      house-surgeon or a house-physician—little more than a senior"
2852	"      student. And he left five years ago—the date is on the stick. So"
2852	"      your grave, middle-aged family practitioner vanishes into thin"
2852	"      air, my dear Watson, and there emerges a young fellow under"
2852	"      thirty, amiable, unambitious, absent-minded, and the possessor of"
2852	"      a favourite dog, which I should describe roughly as being larger"
2852	"      than a terrier and smaller than a mastiff.”"
2852	""
2852	"      I laughed incredulously as Sherlock Holmes leaned back in his"
2852	"      settee and blew little wavering rings of smoke up to the ceiling."
2852	""
2852	"      “As to the latter part, I have no means of checking you,” said I,"
2852	"      “but at least it is not difficult to find out a few particulars"
2852	"      about the man’s age and professional career.” From my small"
2852	"      medical shelf I took down the Medical Directory and turned up the"
2852	"      name. There were several Mortimers, but only one who could be our"
2852	"      visitor. I read his record aloud."
2852	""
2852	"        “Mortimer, James, M.R.C.S., 1882, Grimpen, Dartmoor, Devon."
2852	"        House-surgeon, from 1882 to 1884, at Charing Cross Hospital."
2852	"        Winner of the Jackson prize for Comparative Pathology, with"
2852	"        essay entitled ‘Is Disease a Reversion?’  Corresponding member"
2852	"        of the Swedish Pathological Society.  Author of ‘Some Freaks of"
2852	"        Atavism’ (_Lancet_ 1882).  ‘Do We Progress?’ (_Journal of"
2852	"        Psychology_, March, 1883). Medical Officer for the parishes of"
2852	"        Grimpen, Thorsley, and High Barrow.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No mention of that local hunt, Watson,” said Holmes with a"
2852	"      mischievous smile, “but a country doctor, as you very astutely"
2852	"      observed. I think that I am fairly justified in my inferences. As"
2852	"      to the adjectives, I said, if I remember right, amiable,"
2852	"      unambitious, and absent-minded. It is my experience that it is"
2852	"      only an amiable man in this world who receives testimonials, only"
2852	"      an unambitious one who abandons a London career for the country,"
2852	"      and only an absent-minded one who leaves his stick and not his"
2852	"      visiting-card after waiting an hour in your room.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “And the dog?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Has been in the habit of carrying this stick behind his master."
2852	"      Being a heavy stick the dog has held it tightly by the middle,"
2852	"      and the marks of his teeth are very plainly visible. The dog’s"
2852	"      jaw, as shown in the space between these marks, is too broad in"
2852	"      my opinion for a terrier and not broad enough for a mastiff. It"
2852	"      may have been—yes, by Jove, it _is_ a curly-haired spaniel.”"
2852	""
2852	"      He had risen and paced the room as he spoke. Now he halted in the"
2852	"      recess of the window. There was such a ring of conviction in his"
2852	"      voice that I glanced up in surprise."
2852	""
2852	"      “My dear fellow, how can you possibly be so sure of that?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “For the very simple reason that I see the dog himself on our"
2852	"      very door-step, and there is the ring of its owner. Don’t move, I"
2852	"      beg you, Watson. He is a professional brother of yours, and your"
2852	"      presence may be of assistance to me. Now is the dramatic moment"
2852	"      of fate, Watson, when you hear a step upon the stair which is"
2852	"      walking into your life, and you know not whether for good or ill."
2852	"      What does Dr. James Mortimer, the man of science, ask of Sherlock"
2852	"      Holmes, the specialist in crime? Come in!”"
2852	""
2852	"      The appearance of our visitor was a surprise to me, since I had"
2852	"      expected a typical country practitioner. He was a very tall, thin"
2852	"      man, with a long nose like a beak, which jutted out between two"
2852	"      keen, grey eyes, set closely together and sparkling brightly from"
2852	"      behind a pair of gold-rimmed glasses. He was clad in a"
2852	"      professional but rather slovenly fashion, for his frock-coat was"
2852	"      dingy and his trousers frayed. Though young, his long back was"
2852	"      already bowed, and he walked with a forward thrust of his head"
2852	"      and a general air of peering benevolence. As he entered his eyes"
2852	"      fell upon the stick in Holmes’s hand, and he ran towards it with"
2852	"      an exclamation of joy. “I am so very glad,” said he. “I was not"
2852	"      sure whether I had left it here or in the Shipping Office. I"
2852	"      would not lose that stick for the world.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “A presentation, I see,” said Holmes."
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, sir.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “From Charing Cross Hospital?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “From one or two friends there on the occasion of my marriage.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Dear, dear, that’s bad!” said Holmes, shaking his head."
2852	""
2852	"      Dr. Mortimer blinked through his glasses in mild astonishment."
2852	"      “Why was it bad?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Only that you have disarranged our little deductions. Your"
2852	"      marriage, you say?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, sir. I married, and so left the hospital, and with it all"
2852	"      hopes of a consulting practice. It was necessary to make a home"
2852	"      of my own.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Come, come, we are not so far wrong, after all,” said Holmes."
2852	"      “And now, Dr. James Mortimer—”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Mister, sir, Mister—a humble M.R.C.S.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “And a man of precise mind, evidently.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “A dabbler in science, Mr. Holmes, a picker up of shells on the"
2852	"      shores of the great unknown ocean. I presume that it is Mr."
2852	"      Sherlock Holmes whom I am addressing and not—”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, this is my friend Dr. Watson.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Glad to meet you, sir. I have heard your name mentioned in"
2852	"      connection with that of your friend. You interest me very much,"
2852	"      Mr. Holmes. I had hardly expected so dolichocephalic a skull or"
2852	"      such well-marked supra-orbital development. Would you have any"
2852	"      objection to my running my finger along your parietal fissure? A"
2852	"      cast of your skull, sir, until the original is available, would"
2852	"      be an ornament to any anthropological museum. It is not my"
2852	"      intention to be fulsome, but I confess that I covet your skull.”"
2852	""
2852	"      Sherlock Holmes waved our strange visitor into a chair. “You are"
2852	"      an enthusiast in your line of thought, I perceive, sir, as I am"
2852	"      in mine,” said he. “I observe from your forefinger that you make"
2852	"      your own cigarettes. Have no hesitation in lighting one.”"
2852	""
2852	"      The man drew out paper and tobacco and twirled the one up in the"
2852	"      other with surprising dexterity. He had long, quivering fingers"
2852	"      as agile and restless as the antennæ of an insect."
2852	""
2852	"      Holmes was silent, but his little darting glances showed me the"
2852	"      interest which he took in our curious companion. “I presume,"
2852	"      sir,” said he at last, “that it was not merely for the purpose of"
2852	"      examining my skull that you have done me the honour to call here"
2852	"      last night and again today?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, sir, no; though I am happy to have had the opportunity of"
2852	"      doing that as well. I came to you, Mr. Holmes, because I"
2852	"      recognized that I am myself an unpractical man and because I am"
2852	"      suddenly confronted with a most serious and extraordinary"
2852	"      problem. Recognizing, as I do, that you are the second highest"
2852	"      expert in Europe—”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Indeed, sir! May I inquire who has the honour to be the first?”"
2852	"      asked Holmes with some asperity."
2852	""
2852	"      “To the man of precisely scientific mind the work of Monsieur"
2852	"      Bertillon must always appeal strongly.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Then had you not better consult him?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I said, sir, to the precisely scientific mind. But as a"
2852	"      practical man of affairs it is acknowledged that you stand alone."
2852	"      I trust, sir, that I have not inadvertently—”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Just a little,” said Holmes. “I think, Dr. Mortimer, you would"
2852	"      do wisely if without more ado you would kindly tell me plainly"
2852	"      what the exact nature of the problem is in which you demand my"
2852	"      assistance.”"

2852	"<Chapter 2>"
2852	"The Curse of the Baskervilles"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"      “I have in my pocket a manuscript,” said Dr. James Mortimer."
2852	""
2852	"      “I observed it as you entered the room,” said Holmes."
2852	""
2852	"      “It is an old manuscript.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Early eighteenth century, unless it is a forgery.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “How can you say that, sir?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “You have presented an inch or two of it to my examination all"
2852	"      the time that you have been talking. It would be a poor expert"
2852	"      who could not give the date of a document within a decade or so."
2852	"      You may possibly have read my little monograph upon the subject."
2852	"      I put that at 1730.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “The exact date is 1742.” Dr. Mortimer drew it from his"
2852	"      breast-pocket. “This family paper was committed to my care by Sir"
2852	"      Charles Baskerville, whose sudden and tragic death some three"
2852	"      months ago created so much excitement in Devonshire. I may say"
2852	"      that I was his personal friend as well as his medical attendant."
2852	"      He was a strong-minded man, sir, shrewd, practical, and as"
2852	"      unimaginative as I am myself. Yet he took this document very"
2852	"      seriously, and his mind was prepared for just such an end as did"
2852	"      eventually overtake him.”"
2852	""
2852	"      Holmes stretched out his hand for the manuscript and flattened it"
2852	"      upon his knee. “You will observe, Watson, the alternative use of"
2852	"      the long _s_ and the short. It is one of several indications"
2852	"      which enabled me to fix the date.”"
2852	""
2852	"      I looked over his shoulder at the yellow paper and the faded"
2852	"      script. At the head was written: “Baskerville Hall,” and below in"
2852	"      large, scrawling figures: “1742.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “It appears to be a statement of some sort.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, it is a statement of a certain legend which runs in the"
2852	"      Baskerville family.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But I understand that it is something more modern and practical"
2852	"      upon which you wish to consult me?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Most modern. A most practical, pressing matter, which must be"
2852	"      decided within twenty-four hours. But the manuscript is short and"
2852	"      is intimately connected with the affair. With your permission I"
2852	"      will read it to you.”"
2852	""
2852	"      Holmes leaned back in his chair, placed his finger-tips together,"
2852	"      and closed his eyes, with an air of resignation. Dr. Mortimer"
2852	"      turned the manuscript to the light and read in a high, cracking"
2852	"      voice the following curious, old-world narrative:"
2852	""
2852	"        “Of the origin of the Hound of the Baskervilles there have been"
2852	"        many statements, yet as I come in a direct line from Hugo"
2852	"        Baskerville, and as I had the story from my father, who also"
2852	"        had it from his, I have set it down with all belief that it"
2852	"        occurred even as is here set forth. And I would have you"
2852	"        believe, my sons, that the same Justice which punishes sin may"
2852	"        also most graciously forgive it, and that no ban is so heavy"
2852	"        but that by prayer and repentance it may be removed.  Learn"
2852	"        then from this story not to fear the fruits of the past, but"
2852	"        rather to be circumspect in the future, that those foul"
2852	"        passions whereby our family has suffered so grievously may not"
2852	"        again be loosed to our undoing."
2852	""
2852	"            “Know then that in the time of the Great Rebellion (the"
2852	"            history of which by the learned Lord Clarendon I most"
2852	"            earnestly commend to your attention) this Manor of"
2852	"            Baskerville was held by Hugo of that name, nor can it be"
2852	"            gainsaid that he was a most wild, profane, and godless man."
2852	"             This, in truth, his neighbours might have pardoned, seeing"
2852	"            that saints have never flourished in those parts, but there"
2852	"            was in him a certain wanton and cruel humour which made his"
2852	"            name a by-word through the West.  It chanced that this Hugo"
2852	"            came to love (if, indeed, so dark a passion may be known"
2852	"            under so bright a name) the daughter of a yeoman who held"
2852	"            lands near the Baskerville estate. But the young maiden,"
2852	"            being discreet and of good repute, would ever avoid him,"
2852	"            for she feared his evil name.  So it came to pass that one"
2852	"            Michaelmas this Hugo, with five or six of his idle and"
2852	"            wicked companions, stole down upon the farm and carried off"
2852	"            the maiden, her father and brothers being from home, as he"
2852	"            well knew.  When they had brought her to the Hall the"
2852	"            maiden was placed in an upper chamber, while Hugo and his"
2852	"            friends sat down to a long carouse, as was their nightly"
2852	"            custom.  Now, the poor lass upstairs was like to have her"
2852	"            wits turned at the singing and shouting and terrible oaths"
2852	"            which came up to her from below, for they say that the"
2852	"            words used by Hugo Baskerville, when he was in wine, were"
2852	"            such as might blast the man who said them.  At last in the"
2852	"            stress of her fear she did that which might have daunted"
2852	"            the bravest or most active man, for by the aid of the"
2852	"            growth of ivy which covered (and still covers) the south"
2852	"            wall she came down from under the eaves, and so homeward"
2852	"            across the moor, there being three leagues betwixt the Hall"
2852	"            and her father’s farm."
2852	""
2852	"            “It chanced that some little time later Hugo left his"
2852	"            guests to carry food and drink—with other worse things,"
2852	"            perchance—to his captive, and so found the cage empty and"
2852	"            the bird escaped.  Then, as it would seem, he became as one"
2852	"            that hath a devil, for, rushing down the stairs into the"
2852	"            dining-hall, he sprang upon the great table, flagons and"
2852	"            trenchers flying before him, and he cried aloud before all"
2852	"            the company that he would that very night render his body"
2852	"            and soul to the Powers of Evil if he might but overtake the"
2852	"            wench.  And while the revellers stood aghast at the fury of"
2852	"            the man, one more wicked or, it may be, more drunken than"
2852	"            the rest, cried out that they should put the hounds upon"
2852	"            her.  Whereat Hugo ran from the house, crying to his grooms"
2852	"            that they should saddle his mare and unkennel the pack, and"
2852	"            giving the hounds a kerchief of the maid’s, he swung them"
2852	"            to the line, and so off full cry in the moonlight over the"
2852	"            moor."
2852	""
2852	"            “Now, for some space the revellers stood agape, unable to"
2852	"            understand all that had been done in such haste.  But anon"
2852	"            their bemused wits awoke to the nature of the deed which"
2852	"            was like to be done upon the moorlands.  Everything was now"
2852	"            in an uproar, some calling for their pistols, some for"
2852	"            their horses, and some for another flask of wine.  But at"
2852	"            length some sense came back to their crazed minds, and the"
2852	"            whole of them, thirteen in number, took horse and started"
2852	"            in pursuit.  The moon shone clear above them, and they rode"
2852	"            swiftly abreast, taking that course which the maid must"
2852	"            needs have taken if she were to reach her own home."
2852	""
2852	"            “They had gone a mile or two when they passed one of the"
2852	"            night shepherds upon the moorlands, and they cried to him"
2852	"            to know if he had seen the hunt.  And the man, as the story"
2852	"            goes, was so crazed with fear that he could scarce speak,"
2852	"            but at last he said that he had indeed seen the unhappy"
2852	"            maiden, with the hounds upon her track.  ‘But I have seen"
2852	"            more than that,’ said he, ‘for Hugo Baskerville passed me"
2852	"            upon his black mare, and there ran mute behind him such a"
2852	"            hound of hell as God forbid should ever be at my heels.’ "
2852	"            So the drunken squires cursed the shepherd and rode onward."
2852	"             But soon their skins turned cold, for there came a"
2852	"            galloping across the moor, and the black mare, dabbled with"
2852	"            white froth, went past with trailing bridle and empty"
2852	"            saddle.  Then the revellers rode close together, for a"
2852	"            great fear was on them, but they still followed over the"
2852	"            moor, though each, had he been alone, would have been right"
2852	"            glad to have turned his horse’s head.  Riding slowly in"
2852	"            this fashion they came at last upon the hounds.  These,"
2852	"            though known for their valour and their breed, were"
2852	"            whimpering in a cluster at the head of a deep dip or goyal,"
2852	"            as we call it, upon the moor, some slinking away and some,"
2852	"            with starting hackles and staring eyes, gazing down the"
2852	"            narrow valley before them."
2852	""
2852	"            “The company had come to a halt, more sober men, as you may"
2852	"            guess, than when they started.  The most of them would by"
2852	"            no means advance, but three of them, the boldest, or it may"
2852	"            be the most drunken, rode forward down the goyal. Now, it"
2852	"            opened into a broad space in which stood two of those great"
2852	"            stones, still to be seen there, which were set by certain"
2852	"            forgotten peoples in the days of old. The moon was shining"
2852	"            bright upon the clearing, and there in the centre lay the"
2852	"            unhappy maid where she had fallen, dead of fear and of"
2852	"            fatigue.  But it was not the sight of her body, nor yet was"
2852	"            it that of the body of Hugo Baskerville lying near her,"
2852	"            which raised the hair upon the heads of these three"
2852	"            dare-devil roysterers, but it was that, standing over Hugo,"
2852	"            and plucking at his throat, there stood a foul thing, a"

2852	"            any hound that ever mortal eye has rested upon.  And even"
2852	"            as they looked the thing tore the throat out of Hugo"
2852	"            Baskerville, on which, as it turned its blazing eyes and"
2852	"            dripping jaws upon them, the three shrieked with fear and"
2852	"            rode for dear life, still screaming, across the moor.  One,"
2852	"            it is said, died that very night of what he had seen, and"
2852	"            the other twain were but broken men for the rest of their"
2852	"            days."
2852	""
2852	"            “Such is the tale, my sons, of the coming of the hound"

2852	"            clearly known hath less terror than that which is but"

2852	"            the family have been unhappy in their deaths, which have"

2852	"            exalted."

2852	"        “The recent sudden death of Sir Charles Baskerville, whose name"
2852	"        has been mentioned as the probable Liberal candidate for"
2852	"        Mid-Devon at the next election, has cast a gloom over the"
2852	"        county.  Though Sir Charles had resided at Baskerville Hall for"
2852	"        a comparatively short period his amiability of character and"
2852	"        extreme generosity had won the affection and respect of all who"
2852	"        had been brought into contact with him.  In these days of"
2852	"        _nouveaux riches_ it is refreshing to find a case where the"
2852	"        scion of an old county family which has fallen upon evil days"
2852	"        is able to make his own fortune and to bring it back with him"
2852	"        to restore the fallen grandeur of his line.  Sir Charles, as is"
2852	"        well known, made large sums of money in South African"
2852	"        speculation. More wise than those who go on until the wheel"
2852	"        turns against them, he realised his gains and returned to"
2852	"        England with them.  It is only two years since he took up his"
2852	"        residence at Baskerville Hall, and it is common talk how large"
2852	"        were those schemes of reconstruction and improvement which have"
2852	"        been interrupted by his death.  Being himself childless, it was"
2852	"        his openly expressed desire that the whole countryside should,"
2852	"        within his own lifetime, profit by his good fortune, and many"
2852	"        will have personal reasons for bewailing his untimely end.  His"
2852	"        generous donations to local and county charities have been"
2852	"        frequently chronicled in these columns."

2852	"            “The circumstances connected with the death of Sir Charles"
2852	"            cannot be said to have been entirely cleared up by the"

2852	"            those rumours to which local superstition has given rise."
2852	"            There is no reason whatever to suspect foul play, or to"
2852	"            imagine that death could be from any but natural causes."
2852	"            Sir Charles was a widower, and a man who may be said to"
2852	"            have been in some ways of an eccentric habit of mind. In"
2852	"            spite of his considerable wealth he was simple in his"
2852	"            personal tastes, and his indoor servants at Baskerville"
2852	"            Hall consisted of a married couple named Barrymore, the"
2852	"            husband acting as butler and the wife as housekeeper. Their"
2852	"            evidence, corroborated by that of several friends, tends to"
2852	"            show that Sir Charles’s health has for some time been"
2852	"            impaired, and points especially to some affection of the"
2852	"            heart, manifesting itself in changes of colour,"
2852	"            breathlessness, and acute attacks of nervous depression."
2852	"            Dr. James Mortimer, the friend and medical attendant of the"
2852	"            deceased, has given evidence to the same effect."
2852	""
2852	"            “The facts of the case are simple.  Sir Charles Baskerville"
2852	"            was in the habit every night before going to bed of walking"
2852	"            down the famous yew alley of Baskerville Hall.  The"
2852	"            evidence of the Barrymores shows that this had been his"
2852	"            custom. On the fourth of May Sir Charles had declared his"
2852	"            intention of starting next day for London, and had ordered"
2852	"            Barrymore to prepare his luggage.  That night he went out"
2852	"            as usual for his nocturnal walk, in the course of which he"
2852	"            was in the habit of smoking a cigar.  He never returned. "
2852	"            At twelve o’clock Barrymore, finding the hall door still"
2852	"            open, became alarmed, and, lighting a lantern, went in"
2852	"            search of his master.  The day had been wet, and Sir"
2852	"            Charles’s footmarks were easily traced down the alley. "
2852	"            Halfway down this walk there is a gate which leads out on"
2852	"            to the moor. There were indications that Sir Charles had"
2852	"            stood for some little time here.  He then proceeded down"
2852	"            the alley, and it was at the far end of it that his body"
2852	"            was discovered. One fact which has not been explained is"
2852	"            the statement of Barrymore that his master’s footprints"
2852	"            altered their character from the time that he passed the"
2852	"            moor-gate, and that he appeared from thence onward to have"
2852	"            been walking upon his toes.  One Murphy, a gipsy"
2852	"            horse-dealer, was on the moor at no great distance at the"
2852	"            time, but he appears by his own confession to have been the"
2852	"            worse for drink. He declares that he heard cries but is"
2852	"            unable to state from what direction they came.  No signs of"
2852	"            violence were to be discovered upon Sir Charles’s person,"
2852	"            and though the doctor’s evidence pointed to an almost"
2852	"            incredible facial distortion—so great that Dr. Mortimer"
2852	"            refused at first to believe that it was indeed his friend"
2852	"            and patient who lay before him—it was explained that that"
2852	"            is a symptom which is not unusual in cases of dyspnœa and"
2852	"            death from cardiac exhaustion.  This explanation was borne"
2852	"            out by the post-mortem examination, which showed"
2852	"            long-standing organic disease, and the coroner’s jury"
2852	"            returned a verdict in accordance with the medical evidence."
2852	"             It is well that this is so, for it is obviously of the"
2852	"            utmost importance that Sir Charles’s heir should settle at"
2852	"            the Hall and continue the good work which has been so sadly"
2852	"            interrupted.  Had the prosaic finding of the coroner not"
2852	"            finally put an end to the romantic stories which have been"
2852	"            whispered in connection with the affair, it might have been"
2852	"            difficult to find a tenant for Baskerville Hall.  It is"
2852	"            understood that the next of kin is Mr. Henry Baskerville,"
2852	"            if he be still alive, the son of Sir Charles Baskerville’s"
2852	"            younger brother.  The young man when last heard of was in"
2852	"            America, and inquiries are being instituted with a view to"
2852	"            informing him of his good fortune.”"
2852	""
2852	"      Dr. Mortimer refolded his paper and replaced it in his pocket."
2852	"      “Those are the public facts, Mr. Holmes, in connection with the"

2852	"      “The moor is very sparsely inhabited, and those who live near"

2852	""
2852	"      “I can well remember driving up to his house in the evening some"

2852	""
2852	"      “It was at my advice that Sir Charles was about to go to London."
2852	"      His heart was, I knew, affected, and the constant anxiety in"

2852	"      He was carefully examining the foolscap, upon which the words"

2852	"<Chapter 5>"
2852	"Three Broken Threads"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"      Sherlock Holmes had, in a very remarkable degree, the power of"
2852	"      detaching his mind at will. For two hours the strange business in"
2852	"      which we had been involved appeared to be forgotten, and he was"
2852	"      entirely absorbed in the pictures of the modern Belgian masters."
2852	"      He would talk of nothing but art, of which he had the crudest"

2852	"      the Northumberland Hotel."
2852	""

2852	"      event, for station-master and porters clustered round us to carry"
2852	"      out our luggage. It was a sweet, simple country spot, but I was"
2852	"      surprised to observe that by the gate there stood two soldierly"
2852	"      men in dark uniforms who leaned upon their short rifles and"
2852	"      glanced keenly at us as we passed. The coachman, a hard-faced,"
2852	"      gnarled little fellow, saluted Sir Henry Baskerville, and in a"
2852	"      few minutes we were flying swiftly down the broad, white road."
2852	"      Rolling pasture lands curved upward on either side of us, and old"
2852	"      gabled houses peeped out from amid the thick green foliage, but"
2852	"      behind the peaceful and sunlit countryside there rose ever, dark"
2852	"      against the evening sky, the long, gloomy curve of the moor,"
2852	"      broken by the jagged and sinister hills."
2852	""
2852	"      The wagonette swung round into a side road, and we curved upward"
2852	"      through deep lanes worn by centuries of wheels, high banks on"
2852	"      either side, heavy with dripping moss and fleshy hart’s-tongue"

2852	"      fluttered down upon us as we passed. The rattle of our wheels"
2852	"      died away as we drove through drifts of rotting vegetation—sad"

2852	"      carriage of the returning heir of the Baskervilles."

2852	"      “Halloa!” cried Dr. Mortimer, “what is this?”"
2852	""
2852	"      A steep curve of heath-clad land, an outlying spur of the moor,"
2852	"      lay in front of us. On the summit, hard and clear like an"
2852	"      equestrian statue upon its pedestal, was a mounted soldier, dark"
2852	"      and stern, his rifle poised ready over his forearm. He was"
2852	"      watching the road along which we travelled."
2852	""
2852	"      “What is this, Perkins?” asked Dr. Mortimer."
2852	""
2852	"      Our driver half turned in his seat. “There’s a convict escaped"
2852	"      from Princetown, sir. He’s been out three days now, and the"
2852	"      warders watch every road and every station, but they’ve had no"
2852	"      sight of him yet. The farmers about here don’t like it, sir, and"
2852	"      that’s a fact.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, I understand that they get five pounds if they can give"
2852	"      information.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, sir, but the chance of five pounds is but a poor thing"
2852	"      compared to the chance of having your throat cut. You see, it"
2852	"      isn’t like any ordinary convict. This is a man that would stick"
2852	"      at nothing.”"

2852	""

2852	""
2852	"      I remembered the case well, for it was one in which Holmes had"
2852	"      taken an interest on account of the peculiar ferocity of the"
2852	"      crime and the wanton brutality which had marked all the actions"
2852	"      of the assassin. The commutation of his death sentence had been"

2852	"      his conduct. Our wagonette had topped a rise and in front of us"

2852	""
2852	"      We had left the fertile country behind and beneath us. We looked"

2852	"      creeper to break its harsh outline. Suddenly we looked down into"

2852	"      had been twisted and bent by the fury of years of storm. Two"
2852	"      high, narrow towers rose over the trees. The driver pointed with"
2852	"      his whip."
2852	""
2852	"      “Baskerville Hall,” said he."
2852	""
2852	"      Its master had risen and was staring with flushed cheeks and"
2852	"      shining eyes. A few minutes later we had reached the lodge-gates,"
2852	"      a maze of fantastic tracery in wrought iron, with weather-bitten"

2852	""

2852	"      were again hushed amid the leaves, and the old trees shot their"

2852	"      like a ghost at the farther end."
2852	""
2852	"      “Was it here?” he asked in a low voice."
2852	""
2852	"      “No, no, the yew alley is on the other side.”"
2852	""
2852	"      The young heir glanced round with a gloomy face."
2852	""

2852	"      and you won’t know it again, with a thousand candle-power Swan"

2852	"      and was certainly in the house. For half an hour I waited with"
2852	"      every nerve on the alert, but there came no other sound save the"
2852	"      chiming clock and the rustle of the ivy on the wall."
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"<Chapter 7>"
2852	"The Stapletons of Merripit House"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"      The fresh beauty of the following morning did something to efface"
2852	"      from our minds the grim and grey impression which had been left"
2852	"      upon both of us by our first experience of Baskerville Hall. As"
2852	"      Sir Henry and I sat at breakfast the sunlight flooded in through"
2852	"      the high mullioned windows, throwing watery patches of colour"
2852	"      from the coats of arms which covered them. The dark panelling"
2852	"      glowed like bronze in the golden rays, and it was hard to realise"
2852	"      that this was indeed the chamber which had struck such a gloom"
2852	"      into our souls upon the evening before."
2852	""
2852	"      “I guess it is ourselves and not the house that we have to"
2852	"      blame!” said the baronet. “We were tired with our journey and"
2852	"      chilled by our drive, so we took a grey view of the place. Now we"
2852	"      are fresh and well, so it is all cheerful once more.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “And yet it was not entirely a question of imagination,” I"
2852	"      answered. “Did you, for example, happen to hear someone, a woman"
2852	"      I think, sobbing in the night?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “That is curious, for I did when I was half asleep fancy that I"
2852	"      heard something of the sort. I waited quite a time, but there was"
2852	"      no more of it, so I concluded that it was all a dream.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I heard it distinctly, and I am sure that it was really the sob"
2852	"      of a woman.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “We must ask about this right away.” He rang the bell and asked"
2852	"      Barrymore whether he could account for our experience. It seemed"
2852	"      to me that the pallid features of the butler turned a shade paler"
2852	"      still as he listened to his master’s question."
2852	""
2852	"      “There are only two women in the house, Sir Henry,” he answered."
2852	"      “One is the scullery-maid, who sleeps in the other wing. The"
2852	"      other is my wife, and I can answer for it that the sound could"
2852	"      not have come from her.”"
2852	""
2852	"      And yet he lied as he said it, for it chanced that after"
2852	"      breakfast I met Mrs. Barrymore in the long corridor with the sun"
2852	"      full upon her face. She was a large, impassive, heavy-featured"
2852	"      woman with a stern set expression of mouth. But her telltale eyes"
2852	"      were red and glanced at me from between swollen lids. It was she,"
2852	"      then, who wept in the night, and if she did so her husband must"
2852	"      know it. Yet he had taken the obvious risk of discovery in"

2852	"      she weep so bitterly? Already round this pale-faced, handsome,"

2852	"      It seemed hopeless to pursue the inquiry any farther, but it was"
2852	"      clear that in spite of Holmes’s ruse we had no proof that"

2852	"      might have had a fatal effect upon his diseased heart. I fancy"
2852	"      that he really did see something of the kind upon that last night"
2852	"      in the yew alley. I feared that some disaster might occur, for I"
2852	"      was very fond of the old man, and I knew that his heart was"
2852	"      weak.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “How did you know that?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “My friend Mortimer told me.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “You think, then, that some dog pursued Sir Charles, and that he"
2852	"      died of fright in consequence?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Have you any better explanation?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I have not come to any conclusion.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Has Mr. Sherlock Holmes?”"
2852	""
2852	"      The words took away my breath for an instant but a glance at the"
2852	"      placid face and steadfast eyes of my companion showed that no"
2852	"      surprise was intended."
2852	""
2852	"      “It is useless for us to pretend that we do not know you, Dr."
2852	"      Watson,” said he. “The records of your detective have reached us"
2852	"      here, and you could not celebrate him without being known"
2852	"      yourself. When Mortimer told me your name he could not deny your"
2852	"      identity. If you are here, then it follows that Mr. Sherlock"
2852	"      Holmes is interesting himself in the matter, and I am naturally"
2852	"      curious to know what view he may take.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I am afraid that I cannot answer that question.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “May I ask if he is going to honour us with a visit himself?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “He cannot leave town at present. He has other cases which engage"
2852	"      his attention.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “What a pity! He might throw some light on that which is so dark"
2852	"      to us. But as to your own researches, if there is any possible"
2852	"      way in which I can be of service to you I trust that you will"

2852	"      suspicions or how you propose to investigate the case, I might"
2852	"      perhaps even now give you some aid or advice.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I assure you that I am simply here upon a visit to my friend,"
2852	"      Sir Henry, and that I need no help of any kind.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Excellent!” said Stapleton. “You are perfectly right to be wary"
2852	"      and discreet. I am justly reproved for what I feel was an"
2852	"      unjustifiable intrusion, and I promise you that I will not"
2852	"      mention the matter again.”"
2852	""
2852	"      We had come to a point where a narrow grassy path struck off from"
2852	"      the road and wound away across the moor. A steep,"
2852	"      boulder-sprinkled hill lay upon the right which had in bygone"
2852	"      days been cut into a granite quarry. The face which was turned"
2852	"      towards us formed a dark cliff, with ferns and brambles growing"
2852	"      in its niches. From over a distant rise there floated a grey"
2852	"      plume of smoke."
2852	""
2852	"      “A moderate walk along this moor-path brings us to Merripit"
2852	"      House,” said he. “Perhaps you will spare an hour that I may have"
2852	"      the pleasure of introducing you to my sister.”"
2852	""
2852	"      My first thought was that I should be by Sir Henry’s side. But"
2852	"      then I remembered the pile of papers and bills with which his"
2852	"      study table was littered. It was certain that I could not help"
2852	"      with those. And Holmes had expressly said that I should study the"
2852	"      neighbours upon the moor. I accepted Stapleton’s invitation, and"
2852	"      we turned together down the path."
2852	""
2852	"      “It is a wonderful place, the moor,” said he, looking round over"
2852	"      the undulating downs, long green rollers, with crests of jagged"
2852	"      granite foaming up into fantastic surges. “You never tire of the"
2852	"      moor. You cannot think the wonderful secrets which it contains."
2852	"      It is so vast, and so barren, and so mysterious.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “You know it well, then?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I have only been here two years. The residents would call me a"
2852	"      newcomer. We came shortly after Sir Charles settled. But my"
2852	"      tastes led me to explore every part of the country round, and I"
2852	"      should think that there are few men who know it better than I"
2852	"      do.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Is it hard to know?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Very hard. You see, for example, this great plain to the north"
2852	"      here with the queer hills breaking out of it. Do you observe"
2852	"      anything remarkable about that?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “It would be a rare place for a gallop.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “You would naturally think so and the thought has cost several"
2852	"      their lives before now. You notice those bright green spots"
2852	"      scattered thickly over it?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, they seem more fertile than the rest.”"
2852	""
2852	"      Stapleton laughed. “That is the great Grimpen Mire,” said he. “A"
2852	"      false step yonder means death to man or beast. Only yesterday I"
2852	"      saw one of the moor ponies wander into it. He never came out. I"
2852	"      saw his head for quite a long time craning out of the bog-hole,"
2852	"      but it sucked him down at last. Even in dry seasons it is a"
2852	"      danger to cross it, but after these autumn rains it is an awful"
2852	"      place. And yet I can find my way to the very heart of it and"
2852	"      return alive. By George, there is another of those miserable"
2852	"      ponies!”"
2852	""
2852	"      Something brown was rolling and tossing among the green sedges."
2852	"      Then a long, agonised, writhing neck shot upward and a dreadful"
2852	"      cry echoed over the moor. It turned me cold with horror, but my"
2852	"      companion’s nerves seemed to be stronger than mine."
2852	""
2852	"      “It’s gone!” said he. “The mire has him. Two in two days, and"
2852	"      many more, perhaps, for they get in the way of going there in the"
2852	"      dry weather and never know the difference until the mire has them"
2852	"      in its clutches. It’s a bad place, the great Grimpen Mire.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “And you say you can penetrate it?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, there are one or two paths which a very active man can"
2852	"      take. I have found them out.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But why should you wish to go into so horrible a place?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, you see the hills beyond? They are really islands cut off"
2852	"      on all sides by the impassable mire, which has crawled round them"
2852	"      in the course of years. That is where the rare plants and the"
2852	"      butterflies are, if you have the wit to reach them.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I shall try my luck some day.”"
2852	""
2852	"      He looked at me with a surprised face. “For God’s sake put such"
2852	"      an idea out of your mind,” said he. “Your blood would be upon my"
2852	"      head. I assure you that there would not be the least chance of"
2852	"      your coming back alive. It is only by remembering certain complex"
2852	"      landmarks that I am able to do it.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Halloa!” I cried. “What is that?”"
2852	""
2852	"      A long, low moan, indescribably sad, swept over the moor. It"
2852	"      filled the whole air, and yet it was impossible to say whence it"
2852	"      came. From a dull murmur it swelled into a deep roar, and then"
2852	"      sank back into a melancholy, throbbing murmur once again."
2852	"      Stapleton looked at me with a curious expression in his face."
2852	""
2852	"      “Queer place, the moor!” said he."
2852	""
2852	"      “But what is it?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “The peasants say it is the Hound of the Baskervilles calling for"
2852	"      its prey. I’ve heard it once or twice before, but never quite so"
2852	"      loud.”"
2852	""
2852	"      I looked round, with a chill of fear in my heart, at the huge"
2852	"      swelling plain, mottled with the green patches of rushes. Nothing"
2852	"      stirred over the vast expanse save a pair of ravens, which"
2852	"      croaked loudly from a tor behind us."
2852	""
2852	"      “You are an educated man. You don’t believe such nonsense as"
2852	"      that?” said I. “What do you think is the cause of so strange a"
2852	"      sound?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Bogs make queer noises sometimes. It’s the mud settling, or the"
2852	"      water rising, or something.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, no, that was a living voice.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, perhaps it was. Did you ever hear a bittern booming?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, I never did.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “It’s a very rare bird—practically extinct—in England now, but"
2852	"      all things are possible upon the moor. Yes, I should not be"
2852	"      surprised to learn that what we have heard is the cry of the last"
2852	"      of the bitterns.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “It’s the weirdest, strangest thing that ever I heard in my"
2852	"      life.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, it’s rather an uncanny place altogether. Look at the"
2852	"      hillside yonder. What do you make of those?”"
2852	""
2852	"      The whole steep slope was covered with grey circular rings of"
2852	"      stone, a score of them at least."
2852	""
2852	"      “What are they? Sheep-pens?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, they are the homes of our worthy ancestors. Prehistoric man"
2852	"      lived thickly on the moor, and as no one in particular has lived"
2852	"      there since, we find all his little arrangements exactly as he"
2852	"      left them. These are his wigwams with the roofs off. You can even"
2852	"      see his hearth and his couch if you have the curiosity to go"
2852	"      inside."
2852	""
2852	"      “But it is quite a town. When was it inhabited?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Neolithic man—no date.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “What did he do?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “He grazed his cattle on these slopes, and he learned to dig for"
2852	"      tin when the bronze sword began to supersede the stone axe. Look"
2852	"      at the great trench in the opposite hill. That is his mark. Yes,"
2852	"      you will find some very singular points about the moor, Dr."
2852	"      Watson. Oh, excuse me an instant! It is surely Cyclopides.”"
2852	""
2852	"      A small fly or moth had fluttered across our path, and in an"
2852	"      instant Stapleton was rushing with extraordinary energy and speed"
2852	"      in pursuit of it. To my dismay the creature flew straight for the"
2852	"      great mire, and my acquaintance never paused for an instant,"
2852	"      bounding from tuft to tuft behind it, his green net waving in the"
2852	"      air. His grey clothes and jerky, zigzag, irregular progress made"
2852	"      him not unlike some huge moth himself. I was standing watching"
2852	"      his pursuit with a mixture of admiration for his extraordinary"
2852	"      activity and fear lest he should lose his footing in the"
2852	"      treacherous mire, when I heard the sound of steps and, turning"
2852	"      round, found a woman near me upon the path. She had come from the"
2852	"      direction in which the plume of smoke indicated the position of"
2852	"      Merripit House, but the dip of the moor had hid her until she was"
2852	"      quite close."
2852	""
2852	"      I could not doubt that this was the Miss Stapleton of whom I had"
2852	"      been told, since ladies of any sort must be few upon the moor,"
2852	"      and I remembered that I had heard someone describe her as being a"
2852	"      beauty. The woman who approached me was certainly that, and of a"
2852	"      most uncommon type. There could not have been a greater contrast"
2852	"      between brother and sister, for Stapleton was neutral tinted,"
2852	"      with light hair and grey eyes, while she was darker than any"
2852	"      brunette whom I have seen in England—slim, elegant, and tall. She"
2852	"      had a proud, finely cut face, so regular that it might have"
2852	"      seemed impassive were it not for the sensitive mouth and the"
2852	"      beautiful dark, eager eyes. With her perfect figure and elegant"
2852	"      dress she was, indeed, a strange apparition upon a lonely"
2852	"      moorland path. Her eyes were on her brother as I turned, and then"
2852	"      she quickened her pace towards me. I had raised my hat and was"
2852	"      about to make some explanatory remark when her own words turned"
2852	"      all my thoughts into a new channel."
2852	""
2852	"      “Go back!” she said. “Go straight back to London, instantly.”"
2852	""
2852	"      I could only stare at her in stupid surprise. Her eyes blazed at"
2852	"      me, and she tapped the ground impatiently with her foot."
2852	""
2852	"      “Why should I go back?” I asked."
2852	""
2852	"      “I cannot explain.” She spoke in a low, eager voice, with a"
2852	"      curious lisp in her utterance. “But for God’s sake do what I ask"
2852	"      you. Go back and never set foot upon the moor again.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But I have only just come.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Man, man!” she cried. “Can you not tell when a warning is for"
2852	"      your own good? Go back to London! Start tonight! Get away from"
2852	"      this place at all costs! Hush, my brother is coming! Not a word"
2852	"      of what I have said. Would you mind getting that orchid for me"
2852	"      among the mare’s-tails yonder? We are very rich in orchids on the"
2852	"      moor, though, of course, you are rather late to see the beauties"
2852	"      of the place.”"
2852	""
2852	"      Stapleton had abandoned the chase and came back to us breathing"
2852	"      hard and flushed with his exertions."
2852	""
2852	"      “Halloa, Beryl!” said he, and it seemed to me that the tone of"
2852	"      his greeting was not altogether a cordial one."
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, Jack, you are very hot.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, I was chasing a Cyclopides. He is very rare and seldom"
2852	"      found in the late autumn. What a pity that I should have missed"
2852	"      him!” He spoke unconcernedly, but his small light eyes glanced"
2852	"      incessantly from the girl to me."
2852	""
2852	"      “You have introduced yourselves, I can see.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes. I was telling Sir Henry that it was rather late for him to"
2852	"      see the true beauties of the moor.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Why, who do you think this is?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I imagine that it must be Sir Henry Baskerville.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, no,” said I. “Only a humble commoner, but his friend. My"
2852	"      name is Dr. Watson.”"
2852	""
2852	"      A flush of vexation passed over her expressive face. “We have"
2852	"      been talking at cross purposes,” said she."
2852	""
2852	"      “Why, you had not very much time for talk,” her brother remarked"
2852	"      with the same questioning eyes."
2852	""
2852	"      “I talked as if Dr. Watson were a resident instead of being"
2852	"      merely a visitor,” said she. “It cannot much matter to him"
2852	"      whether it is early or late for the orchids. But you will come"
2852	"      on, will you not, and see Merripit House?”"
2852	""
2852	"      A short walk brought us to it, a bleak moorland house, once the"
2852	"      farm of some grazier in the old prosperous days, but now put into"
2852	"      repair and turned into a modern dwelling. An orchard surrounded"
2852	"      it, but the trees, as is usual upon the moor, were stunted and"
2852	"      nipped, and the effect of the whole place was mean and"
2852	"      melancholy. We were admitted by a strange, wizened, rusty-coated"
2852	"      old manservant, who seemed in keeping with the house. Inside,"
2852	"      however, there were large rooms furnished with an elegance in"
2852	"      which I seemed to recognize the taste of the lady. As I looked"
2852	"      from their windows at the interminable granite-flecked moor"
2852	"      rolling unbroken to the farthest horizon I could not but marvel"
