2852	"      beautiful woman to live in such a place."
2852	""
2852	"      “Queer spot to choose, is it not?” said he as if in answer to my"
2852	"      thought. “And yet we manage to make ourselves fairly happy, do we"
2852	"      not, Beryl?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Quite happy,” said she, but there was no ring of conviction in"
2852	"      her words."
2852	""
2852	"      “I had a school,” said Stapleton. “It was in the north country."
2852	"      The work to a man of my temperament was mechanical and"
2852	"      uninteresting, but the privilege of living with youth, of helping"
2852	"      to mould those young minds, and of impressing them with one’s own"
2852	"      character and ideals was very dear to me. However, the fates were"
2852	"      against us. A serious epidemic broke out in the school and three"
2852	"      of the boys died. It never recovered from the blow, and much of"
2852	"      my capital was irretrievably swallowed up. And yet, if it were"
2852	"      not for the loss of the charming companionship of the boys, I"
2852	"      could rejoice over my own misfortune, for, with my strong tastes"
2852	"      for botany and zoology, I find an unlimited field of work here,"
2852	"      and my sister is as devoted to Nature as I am. All this, Dr."
2852	"      Watson, has been brought upon your head by your expression as you"
2852	"      surveyed the moor out of our window.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “It certainly did cross my mind that it might be a little"
2852	"      dull—less for you, perhaps, than for your sister.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, no, I am never dull,” said she quickly."
2852	""
2852	"      “We have books, we have our studies, and we have interesting"
2852	"      neighbours. Dr. Mortimer is a most learned man in his own line."
2852	"      Poor Sir Charles was also an admirable companion. We knew him"
2852	"      well and miss him more than I can tell. Do you think that I"
2852	"      should intrude if I were to call this afternoon and make the"
2852	"      acquaintance of Sir Henry?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I am sure that he would be delighted.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Then perhaps you would mention that I propose to do so. We may"
2852	"      in our humble way do something to make things more easy for him"
2852	"      until he becomes accustomed to his new surroundings. Will you"
2852	"      come upstairs, Dr. Watson, and inspect my collection of"
2852	"      Lepidoptera? I think it is the most complete one in the"
2852	"      south-west of England. By the time that you have looked through"
2852	"      them lunch will be almost ready.”"
2852	""
2852	"      But I was eager to get back to my charge. The melancholy of the"
2852	"      moor, the death of the unfortunate pony, the weird sound which"
2852	"      had been associated with the grim legend of the Baskervilles, all"
2852	"      these things tinged my thoughts with sadness. Then on the top of"
2852	"      these more or less vague impressions there had come the definite"
2852	"      and distinct warning of Miss Stapleton, delivered with such"
2852	"      intense earnestness that I could not doubt that some grave and"
2852	"      deep reason lay behind it. I resisted all pressure to stay for"
2852	"      lunch, and I set off at once upon my return journey, taking the"
2852	"      grass-grown path by which we had come."
2852	""
2852	"      It seems, however, that there must have been some short cut for"
2852	"      those who knew it, for before I had reached the road I was"
2852	"      astounded to see Miss Stapleton sitting upon a rock by the side"
2852	"      of the track. Her face was beautifully flushed with her exertions"
2852	"      and she held her hand to her side."
2852	""
2852	"      “I have run all the way in order to cut you off, Dr. Watson,”"
2852	"      said she. “I had not even time to put on my hat. I must not stop,"
2852	"      or my brother may miss me. I wanted to say to you how sorry I am"
2852	"      about the stupid mistake I made in thinking that you were Sir"
2852	"      Henry. Please forget the words I said, which have no application"
2852	"      whatever to you.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But I can’t forget them, Miss Stapleton,” said I. “I am Sir"
2852	"      Henry’s friend, and his welfare is a very close concern of mine."
2852	"      Tell me why it was that you were so eager that Sir Henry should"
2852	"      return to London.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “A woman’s whim, Dr. Watson. When you know me better you will"
2852	"      understand that I cannot always give reasons for what I say or"
2852	"      do.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, no. I remember the thrill in your voice. I remember the look"
2852	"      in your eyes. Please, please, be frank with me, Miss Stapleton,"
2852	"      for ever since I have been here I have been conscious of shadows"
2852	"      all round me. Life has become like that great Grimpen Mire, with"
2852	"      little green patches everywhere into which one may sink and with"
2852	"      no guide to point the track. Tell me then what it was that you"
2852	"      meant, and I will promise to convey your warning to Sir Henry.”"
2852	""
2852	"      An expression of irresolution passed for an instant over her"
2852	"      face, but her eyes had hardened again when she answered me."
2852	""
2852	"      “You make too much of it, Dr. Watson,” said she. “My brother and"
2852	"      I were very much shocked by the death of Sir Charles. We knew him"
2852	"      very intimately, for his favourite walk was over the moor to our"
2852	"      house. He was deeply impressed with the curse which hung over the"
2852	"      family, and when this tragedy came I naturally felt that there"
2852	"      must be some grounds for the fears which he had expressed. I was"
2852	"      distressed therefore when another member of the family came down"
2852	"      to live here, and I felt that he should be warned of the danger"
2852	"      which he will run. That was all which I intended to convey."
2852	""
2852	"      “But what is the danger?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “You know the story of the hound?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I do not believe in such nonsense.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But I do. If you have any influence with Sir Henry, take him"
2852	"      away from a place which has always been fatal to his family. The"
2852	"      world is wide. Why should he wish to live at the place of"
2852	"      danger?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Because it _is_ the place of danger. That is Sir Henry’s nature."
2852	"      I fear that unless you can give me some more definite information"
2852	"      than this it would be impossible to get him to move.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I cannot say anything definite, for I do not know anything"
2852	"      definite.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I would ask you one more question, Miss Stapleton. If you meant"
2852	"      no more than this when you first spoke to me, why should you not"
2852	"      wish your brother to overhear what you said? There is nothing to"
2852	"      which he, or anyone else, could object.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “My brother is very anxious to have the Hall inhabited, for he"
2852	"      thinks it is for the good of the poor folk upon the moor. He"
2852	"      would be very angry if he knew that I have said anything which"
2852	"      might induce Sir Henry to go away. But I have done my duty now"
2852	"      and I will say no more. I must go back, or he will miss me and"
2852	"      suspect that I have seen you. Good-bye!” She turned and had"
2852	"      disappeared in a few minutes among the scattered boulders, while"
2852	"      I, with my soul full of vague fears, pursued my way to"
2852	"      Baskerville Hall."
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"<Chapter 8>"
2852	"First Report of Dr. Watson"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"      From this point onward I will follow the course of events by"
2852	"      transcribing my own letters to Mr. Sherlock Holmes which lie"
2852	"      before me on the table. One page is missing, but otherwise they"
2852	"      are exactly as written and show my feelings and suspicions of the"
2852	"      moment more accurately than my memory, clear as it is upon these"
2852	"      tragic events, can possibly do."
2852	""
2852	"      Baskerville Hall, October 13th."
2852	""
2852	"      MY DEAR HOLMES,"
2852	""
2852	"      My previous letters and telegrams have kept you pretty well up to"
2852	"      date as to all that has occurred in this most God-forsaken corner"
2852	"      of the world. The longer one stays here the more does the spirit"
2852	"      of the moor sink into one’s soul, its vastness, and also its grim"
2852	"      charm. When you are once out upon its bosom you have left all"
2852	"      traces of modern England behind you, but, on the other hand, you"
2852	"      are conscious everywhere of the homes and the work of the"
2852	"      prehistoric people. On all sides of you as you walk are the"
2852	"      houses of these forgotten folk, with their graves and the huge"
2852	"      monoliths which are supposed to have marked their temples. As you"
2852	"      look at their grey stone huts against the scarred hillsides you"
2852	"      leave your own age behind you, and if you were to see a"
2852	"      skin-clad, hairy man crawl out from the low door fitting a"
2852	"      flint-tipped arrow on to the string of his bow, you would feel"
2852	"      that his presence there was more natural than your own. The"
2852	"      strange thing is that they should have lived so thickly on what"
2852	"      must always have been most unfruitful soil. I am no antiquarian,"
2852	"      but I could imagine that they were some unwarlike and harried"
2852	"      race who were forced to accept that which none other would"
2852	"      occupy."
2852	""
2852	"      All this, however, is foreign to the mission on which you sent me"
2852	"      and will probably be very uninteresting to your severely"
2852	"      practical mind. I can still remember your complete indifference"
2852	"      as to whether the sun moved round the earth or the earth round"
2852	"      the sun. Let me, therefore, return to the facts concerning Sir"
2852	"      Henry Baskerville."
2852	""
2852	"      If you have not had any report within the last few days it is"
2852	"      because up to today there was nothing of importance to relate."
2852	"      Then a very surprising circumstance occurred, which I shall tell"
2852	"      you in due course. But, first of all, I must keep you in touch"
2852	"      with some of the other factors in the situation."
2852	""
2852	"      One of these, concerning which I have said little, is the escaped"
2852	"      convict upon the moor. There is strong reason now to believe that"
2852	"      he has got right away, which is a considerable relief to the"
2852	"      lonely householders of this district. A fortnight has passed"
2852	"      since his flight, during which he has not been seen and nothing"
2852	"      has been heard of him. It is surely inconceivable that he could"
2852	"      have held out upon the moor during all that time. Of course, so"
2852	"      far as his concealment goes there is no difficulty at all. Any"
2852	"      one of these stone huts would give him a hiding-place. But there"
2852	"      is nothing to eat unless he were to catch and slaughter one of"
2852	"      the moor sheep. We think, therefore, that he has gone, and the"
2852	"      outlying farmers sleep the better in consequence."
2852	""
2852	"      We are four able-bodied men in this household, so that we could"
2852	"      take good care of ourselves, but I confess that I have had uneasy"
2852	"      moments when I have thought of the Stapletons. They live miles"
2852	"      from any help. There are one maid, an old manservant, the sister,"
2852	"      and the brother, the latter not a very strong man. They would be"
2852	"      helpless in the hands of a desperate fellow like this Notting"
2852	"      Hill criminal if he could once effect an entrance. Both Sir Henry"
2852	"      and I were concerned at their situation, and it was suggested"
2852	"      that Perkins the groom should go over to sleep there, but"
2852	"      Stapleton would not hear of it."
2852	""
2852	"      The fact is that our friend, the baronet, begins to display a"
2852	"      considerable interest in our fair neighbour. It is not to be"
2852	"      wondered at, for time hangs heavily in this lonely spot to an"
2852	"      active man like him, and she is a very fascinating and beautiful"
2852	"      woman. There is something tropical and exotic about her which"
2852	"      forms a singular contrast to her cool and unemotional brother."
2852	"      Yet he also gives the idea of hidden fires. He has certainly a"
2852	"      very marked influence over her, for I have seen her continually"
2852	"      glance at him as she talked as if seeking approbation for what"
2852	"      she said. I trust that he is kind to her. There is a dry glitter"
2852	"      in his eyes and a firm set of his thin lips, which goes with a"
2852	"      positive and possibly a harsh nature. You would find him an"
2852	"      interesting study."
2852	""
2852	"      He came over to call upon Baskerville on that first day, and the"
2852	"      very next morning he took us both to show us the spot where the"
2852	"      legend of the wicked Hugo is supposed to have had its origin. It"
2852	"      was an excursion of some miles across the moor to a place which"
2852	"      is so dismal that it might have suggested the story. We found a"
2852	"      short valley between rugged tors which led to an open, grassy"
2852	"      space flecked over with the white cotton grass. In the middle of"
2852	"      it rose two great stones, worn and sharpened at the upper end"
2852	"      until they looked like the huge corroding fangs of some monstrous"
2852	"      beast. In every way it corresponded with the scene of the old"
2852	"      tragedy. Sir Henry was much interested and asked Stapleton more"
2852	"      than once whether he did really believe in the possibility of the"
2852	"      interference of the supernatural in the affairs of men. He spoke"
2852	"      lightly, but it was evident that he was very much in earnest."
2852	"      Stapleton was guarded in his replies, but it was easy to see that"
2852	"      he said less than he might, and that he would not express his"
2852	"      whole opinion out of consideration for the feelings of the"
2852	"      baronet. He told us of similar cases, where families had suffered"
2852	"      from some evil influence, and he left us with the impression that"
2852	"      he shared the popular view upon the matter."
2852	""
2852	"      On our way back we stayed for lunch at Merripit House, and it was"
2852	"      there that Sir Henry made the acquaintance of Miss Stapleton."
2852	"      From the first moment that he saw her he appeared to be strongly"
2852	"      attracted by her, and I am much mistaken if the feeling was not"
2852	"      mutual. He referred to her again and again on our walk home, and"
2852	"      since then hardly a day has passed that we have not seen"
2852	"      something of the brother and sister. They dine here tonight, and"
2852	"      there is some talk of our going to them next week. One would"
2852	"      imagine that such a match would be very welcome to Stapleton, and"
2852	"      yet I have more than once caught a look of the strongest"
2852	"      disapprobation in his face when Sir Henry has been paying some"
2852	"      attention to his sister. He is much attached to her, no doubt,"
2852	"      and would lead a lonely life without her, but it would seem the"
2852	"      height of selfishness if he were to stand in the way of her"
2852	"      making so brilliant a marriage. Yet I am certain that he does not"
2852	"      wish their intimacy to ripen into love, and I have several times"
2852	"      observed that he has taken pains to prevent them from being"
2852	"      _tête-à-tête_. By the way, your instructions to me never to allow"
2852	"      Sir Henry to go out alone will become very much more onerous if a"
2852	"      love affair were to be added to our other difficulties. My"
2852	"      popularity would soon suffer if I were to carry out your orders"
2852	"      to the letter."
2852	""
2852	"      The other day—Thursday, to be more exact—Dr. Mortimer lunched"
2852	"      with us. He has been excavating a barrow at Long Down and has got"
2852	"      a prehistoric skull which fills him with great joy. Never was"
2852	"      there such a single-minded enthusiast as he! The Stapletons came"
2852	"      in afterwards, and the good doctor took us all to the yew alley"
2852	"      at Sir Henry’s request to show us exactly how everything occurred"
2852	"      upon that fatal night. It is a long, dismal walk, the yew alley,"
2852	"      between two high walls of clipped hedge, with a narrow band of"
2852	"      grass upon either side. At the far end is an old tumble-down"
2852	"      summer-house. Halfway down is the moor-gate, where the old"
2852	"      gentleman left his cigar-ash. It is a white wooden gate with a"
2852	"      latch. Beyond it lies the wide moor. I remembered your theory of"
2852	"      the affair and tried to picture all that had occurred. As the old"
2852	"      man stood there he saw something coming across the moor,"
2852	"      something which terrified him so that he lost his wits and ran"
2852	"      and ran until he died of sheer horror and exhaustion. There was"
2852	"      the long, gloomy tunnel down which he fled. And from what? A"
2852	"      sheep-dog of the moor? Or a spectral hound, black, silent, and"
2852	"      monstrous? Was there a human agency in the matter? Did the pale,"
2852	"      watchful Barrymore know more than he cared to say? It was all dim"
2852	"      and vague, but always there is the dark shadow of crime behind"
2852	"      it."
2852	""
2852	"      One other neighbour I have met since I wrote last. This is Mr."
2852	"      Frankland, of Lafter Hall, who lives some four miles to the south"
2852	"      of us. He is an elderly man, red-faced, white-haired, and"
2852	"      choleric. His passion is for the British law, and he has spent a"
2852	"      large fortune in litigation. He fights for the mere pleasure of"
2852	"      fighting and is equally ready to take up either side of a"
2852	"      question, so that it is no wonder that he has found it a costly"
2852	"      amusement. Sometimes he will shut up a right of way and defy the"
2852	"      parish to make him open it. At others he will with his own hands"
2852	"      tear down some other man’s gate and declare that a path has"
2852	"      existed there from time immemorial, defying the owner to"
2852	"      prosecute him for trespass. He is learned in old manorial and"
2852	"      communal rights, and he applies his knowledge sometimes in favour"
2852	"      of the villagers of Fernworthy and sometimes against them, so"
2852	"      that he is periodically either carried in triumph down the"
2852	"      village street or else burned in effigy, according to his latest"
2852	"      exploit. He is said to have about seven lawsuits upon his hands"
2852	"      at present, which will probably swallow up the remainder of his"
2852	"      fortune and so draw his sting and leave him harmless for the"
2852	"      future. Apart from the law he seems a kindly, good-natured"
2852	"      person, and I only mention him because you were particular that I"
2852	"      should send some description of the people who surround us. He is"
2852	"      curiously employed at present, for, being an amateur astronomer,"
2852	"      he has an excellent telescope, with which he lies upon the roof"
2852	"      of his own house and sweeps the moor all day in the hope of"
2852	"      catching a glimpse of the escaped convict. If he would confine"
2852	"      his energies to this all would be well, but there are rumours"
2852	"      that he intends to prosecute Dr. Mortimer for opening a grave"
2852	"      without the consent of the next of kin because he dug up the"
2852	"      Neolithic skull in the barrow on Long Down. He helps to keep our"
2852	"      lives from being monotonous and gives a little comic relief where"
2852	"      it is badly needed."
2852	""
2852	"      And now, having brought you up to date in the escaped convict,"
2852	"      the Stapletons, Dr. Mortimer, and Frankland, of Lafter Hall, let"
2852	"      me end on that which is most important and tell you more about"
2852	"      the Barrymores, and especially about the surprising development"
2852	"      of last night."
2852	""
2852	"      First of all about the test telegram, which you sent from London"
2852	"      in order to make sure that Barrymore was really here. I have"
2852	"      already explained that the testimony of the postmaster shows that"
2852	"      the test was worthless and that we have no proof one way or the"
2852	"      other. I told Sir Henry how the matter stood, and he at once, in"
2852	"      his downright fashion, had Barrymore up and asked him whether he"
2852	"      had received the telegram himself. Barrymore said that he had."
2852	""
2852	"      “Did the boy deliver it into your own hands?” asked Sir Henry."
2852	""
2852	"      Barrymore looked surprised, and considered for a little time."
2852	""
2852	"      “No,” said he, “I was in the box-room at the time, and my wife"
2852	"      brought it up to me.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Did you answer it yourself?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No; I told my wife what to answer and she went down to write"
2852	"      it.”"
2852	""
2852	"      In the evening he recurred to the subject of his own accord."
2852	""
2852	"      “I could not quite understand the object of your questions this"
2852	"      morning, Sir Henry,” said he. “I trust that they do not mean that"
2852	"      I have done anything to forfeit your confidence?”"
2852	""
2852	"      Sir Henry had to assure him that it was not so and pacify him by"
2852	"      giving him a considerable part of his old wardrobe, the London"
2852	"      outfit having now all arrived."
2852	""
2852	"      Mrs. Barrymore is of interest to me. She is a heavy, solid"
2852	"      person, very limited, intensely respectable, and inclined to be"
2852	"      puritanical. You could hardly conceive a less emotional subject."
2852	"      Yet I have told you how, on the first night here, I heard her"
2852	"      sobbing bitterly, and since then I have more than once observed"
2852	"      traces of tears upon her face. Some deep sorrow gnaws ever at her"
2852	"      heart. Sometimes I wonder if she has a guilty memory which haunts"
2852	"      her, and sometimes I suspect Barrymore of being a domestic"
2852	"      tyrant. I have always felt that there was something singular and"
2852	"      questionable in this man’s character, but the adventure of last"
2852	"      night brings all my suspicions to a head."
2852	""
2852	"      And yet it may seem a small matter in itself. You are aware that"
2852	"      I am not a very sound sleeper, and since I have been on guard in"
2852	"      this house my slumbers have been lighter than ever. Last night,"
2852	"      about two in the morning, I was aroused by a stealthy step"
2852	"      passing my room. I rose, opened my door, and peeped out. A long"
2852	"      black shadow was trailing down the corridor. It was thrown by a"
2852	"      man who walked softly down the passage with a candle held in his"
2852	"      hand. He was in shirt and trousers, with no covering to his feet."
2852	"      I could merely see the outline, but his height told me that it"
2852	"      was Barrymore. He walked very slowly and circumspectly, and there"
2852	"      was something indescribably guilty and furtive in his whole"
2852	"      appearance."
2852	""
2852	"      I have told you that the corridor is broken by the balcony which"
2852	"      runs round the hall, but that it is resumed upon the farther"
2852	"      side. I waited until he had passed out of sight and then I"
2852	"      followed him. When I came round the balcony he had reached the"
2852	"      end of the farther corridor, and I could see from the glimmer of"
2852	"      light through an open door that he had entered one of the rooms."
2852	"      Now, all these rooms are unfurnished and unoccupied so that his"
2852	"      expedition became more mysterious than ever. The light shone"
2852	"      steadily as if he were standing motionless. I crept down the"
2852	"      passage as noiselessly as I could and peeped round the corner of"
2852	"      the door."
2852	""
2852	"      Barrymore was crouching at the window with the candle held"
2852	"      against the glass. His profile was half turned towards me, and"
2852	"      his face seemed to be rigid with expectation as he stared out"
2852	"      into the blackness of the moor. For some minutes he stood"
2852	"      watching intently. Then he gave a deep groan and with an"
2852	"      impatient gesture he put out the light. Instantly I made my way"
2852	"      back to my room, and very shortly came the stealthy steps passing"
2852	"      once more upon their return journey. Long afterwards when I had"
2852	"      fallen into a light sleep I heard a key turn somewhere in a lock,"
2852	"      but I could not tell whence the sound came. What it all means I"
2852	"      cannot guess, but there is some secret business going on in this"
2852	"      house of gloom which sooner or later we shall get to the bottom"
2852	"      of. I do not trouble you with my theories, for you asked me to"
2852	"      furnish you only with facts. I have had a long talk with Sir"
2852	"      Henry this morning, and we have made a plan of campaign founded"
2852	"      upon my observations of last night. I will not speak about it"
2852	"      just now, but it should make my next report interesting reading."
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"<Chapter 9>"
2852	"The Light upon the Moor [Second Report of Dr. Watson]"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"      Baskerville Hall, Oct. 15th."
2852	""
2852	"      MY DEAR HOLMES,"
2852	""
2852	"      If I was compelled to leave you without much news during the"
2852	"      early days of my mission you must acknowledge that I am making up"
2852	"      for lost time, and that events are now crowding thick and fast"
2852	"      upon us. In my last report I ended upon my top note with"
2852	"      Barrymore at the window, and now I have quite a budget already"
2852	"      which will, unless I am much mistaken, considerably surprise you."
2852	"      Things have taken a turn which I could not have anticipated. In"
2852	"      some ways they have within the last forty-eight hours become much"
2852	"      clearer and in some ways they have become more complicated. But I"
2852	"      will tell you all and you shall judge for yourself."
2852	""
2852	"      Before breakfast on the morning following my adventure I went"
2852	"      down the corridor and examined the room in which Barrymore had"
2852	"      been on the night before. The western window through which he had"
2852	"      stared so intently has, I noticed, one peculiarity above all"
2852	"      other windows in the house—it commands the nearest outlook on to"
2852	"      the moor. There is an opening between two trees which enables one"
2852	"      from this point of view to look right down upon it, while from"
2852	"      all the other windows it is only a distant glimpse which can be"
2852	"      obtained. It follows, therefore, that Barrymore, since only this"
2852	"      window would serve the purpose, must have been looking out for"
2852	"      something or somebody upon the moor. The night was very dark, so"
2852	"      that I can hardly imagine how he could have hoped to see anyone."
2852	"      It had struck me that it was possible that some love intrigue was"
2852	"      on foot. That would have accounted for his stealthy movements and"
2852	"      also for the uneasiness of his wife. The man is a"
2852	"      striking-looking fellow, very well equipped to steal the heart of"
2852	"      a country girl, so that this theory seemed to have something to"
2852	"      support it. That opening of the door which I had heard after I"
2852	"      had returned to my room might mean that he had gone out to keep"
2852	"      some clandestine appointment. So I reasoned with myself in the"
2852	"      morning, and I tell you the direction of my suspicions, however"
2852	"      much the result may have shown that they were unfounded."
2852	""
2852	"      But whatever the true explanation of Barrymore’s movements might"
2852	"      be, I felt that the responsibility of keeping them to myself"
2852	"      until I could explain them was more than I could bear. I had an"
2852	"      interview with the baronet in his study after breakfast, and I"
2852	"      told him all that I had seen. He was less surprised than I had"
2852	"      expected."
2852	""
2852	"      “I knew that Barrymore walked about nights, and I had a mind to"
2852	"      speak to him about it,” said he. “Two or three times I have heard"
2852	"      his steps in the passage, coming and going, just about the hour"
2852	"      you name.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Perhaps then he pays a visit every night to that particular"
2852	"      window,” I suggested."
2852	""
2852	"      “Perhaps he does. If so, we should be able to shadow him and see"
2852	"      what it is that he is after. I wonder what your friend Holmes"
2852	"      would do if he were here.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I believe that he would do exactly what you now suggest,” said"
2852	"      I. “He would follow Barrymore and see what he did.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Then we shall do it together.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But surely he would hear us.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “The man is rather deaf, and in any case we must take our chance"
2852	"      of that. We’ll sit up in my room tonight and wait until he"
2852	"      passes.” Sir Henry rubbed his hands with pleasure, and it was"
2852	"      evident that he hailed the adventure as a relief to his somewhat"
2852	"      quiet life upon the moor."
2852	""
2852	"      The baronet has been in communication with the architect who"
2852	"      prepared the plans for Sir Charles, and with a contractor from"
2852	"      London, so that we may expect great changes to begin here soon."
2852	"      There have been decorators and furnishers up from Plymouth, and"
2852	"      it is evident that our friend has large ideas and means to spare"
2852	"      no pains or expense to restore the grandeur of his family. When"
2852	"      the house is renovated and refurnished, all that he will need"
2852	"      will be a wife to make it complete. Between ourselves there are"
2852	"      pretty clear signs that this will not be wanting if the lady is"
2852	"      willing, for I have seldom seen a man more infatuated with a"
2852	"      woman than he is with our beautiful neighbour, Miss Stapleton."
2852	"      And yet the course of true love does not run quite as smoothly as"
2852	"      one would under the circumstances expect. Today, for example, its"
2852	"      surface was broken by a very unexpected ripple, which has caused"
2852	"      our friend considerable perplexity and annoyance."
2852	""
2852	"      After the conversation which I have quoted about Barrymore, Sir"
2852	"      Henry put on his hat and prepared to go out. As a matter of"
2852	"      course I did the same."
2852	""
2852	"      “What, are _you_ coming, Watson?” he asked, looking at me in a"
2852	"      curious way."
2852	""
2852	"      “That depends on whether you are going on the moor,” said I."
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, I am.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, you know what my instructions are. I am sorry to intrude,"
2852	"      but you heard how earnestly Holmes insisted that I should not"
2852	"      leave you, and especially that you should not go alone upon the"
2852	"      moor.”"
2852	""
2852	"      Sir Henry put his hand upon my shoulder with a pleasant smile."
2852	""
2852	"      “My dear fellow,” said he, “Holmes, with all his wisdom, did not"
2852	"      foresee some things which have happened since I have been on the"
2852	"      moor. You understand me? I am sure that you are the last man in"
2852	"      the world who would wish to be a spoil-sport. I must go out"
2852	"      alone.”"
2852	""
2852	"      It put me in a most awkward position. I was at a loss what to say"
2852	"      or what to do, and before I had made up my mind he picked up his"
2852	"      cane and was gone."
2852	""
2852	"      But when I came to think the matter over my conscience reproached"
2852	"      me bitterly for having on any pretext allowed him to go out of my"
2852	"      sight. I imagined what my feelings would be if I had to return to"
2852	"      you and to confess that some misfortune had occurred through my"
2852	"      disregard for your instructions. I assure you my cheeks flushed"
2852	"      at the very thought. It might not even now be too late to"
2852	"      overtake him, so I set off at once in the direction of Merripit"
2852	"      House."
2852	""
2852	"      I hurried along the road at the top of my speed without seeing"
2852	"      anything of Sir Henry, until I came to the point where the moor"
2852	"      path branches off. There, fearing that perhaps I had come in the"
2852	"      wrong direction after all, I mounted a hill from which I could"
2852	"      command a view—the same hill which is cut into the dark quarry."
2852	"      Thence I saw him at once. He was on the moor path about a quarter"
2852	"      of a mile off, and a lady was by his side who could only be Miss"
2852	"      Stapleton. It was clear that there was already an understanding"
2852	"      between them and that they had met by appointment. They were"
2852	"      walking slowly along in deep conversation, and I saw her making"
2852	"      quick little movements of her hands as if she were very earnest"
2852	"      in what she was saying, while he listened intently, and once or"
2852	"      twice shook his head in strong dissent. I stood among the rocks"
2852	"      watching them, very much puzzled as to what I should do next. To"
2852	"      follow them and break into their intimate conversation seemed to"
2852	"      be an outrage, and yet my clear duty was never for an instant to"
2852	"      let him out of my sight. To act the spy upon a friend was a"
2852	"      hateful task. Still, I could see no better course than to observe"
2852	"      him from the hill, and to clear my conscience by confessing to"
2852	"      him afterwards what I had done. It is true that if any sudden"
2852	"      danger had threatened him I was too far away to be of use, and"
2852	"      yet I am sure that you will agree with me that the position was"
2852	"      very difficult, and that there was nothing more which I could do."
2852	""
2852	"      Our friend, Sir Henry, and the lady had halted on the path and"
2852	"      were standing deeply absorbed in their conversation, when I was"
2852	"      suddenly aware that I was not the only witness of their"
2852	"      interview. A wisp of green floating in the air caught my eye, and"
2852	"      another glance showed me that it was carried on a stick by a man"
2852	"      who was moving among the broken ground. It was Stapleton with his"
2852	"      butterfly-net. He was very much closer to the pair than I was,"
2852	"      and he appeared to be moving in their direction. At this instant"
2852	"      Sir Henry suddenly drew Miss Stapleton to his side. His arm was"
2852	"      round her, but it seemed to me that she was straining away from"
2852	"      him with her face averted. He stooped his head to hers, and she"
2852	"      raised one hand as if in protest. Next moment I saw them spring"

2852	"      “No, no.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “And yet it was one thing to laugh about it in London, and it is"
2852	"      another to stand out here in the darkness of the moor and to hear"
2852	"      such a cry as that. And my uncle! There was the footprint of the"
2852	"      hound beside him as he lay. It all fits together. I don’t think"
2852	"      that I am a coward, Watson, but that sound seemed to freeze my"
2852	"      very blood. Feel my hand!”"
2852	""
2852	"      It was as cold as a block of marble."
2852	""
2852	"      “You’ll be all right tomorrow.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I don’t think I’ll get that cry out of my head. What do you"
2852	"      advise that we do now?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Shall we turn back?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, by thunder; we have come out to get our man, and we will do"
2852	"      it. We after the convict, and a hell-hound, as likely as not,"
2852	"      after us. Come on! We’ll see it through if all the fiends of the"
2852	"      pit were loose upon the moor.”"
2852	""
2852	"      We stumbled slowly along in the darkness, with the black loom of"
2852	"      the craggy hills around us, and the yellow speck of light burning"
2852	"      steadily in front. There is nothing so deceptive as the distance"
2852	"      of a light upon a pitch-dark night, and sometimes the glimmer"
2852	"      seemed to be far away upon the horizon and sometimes it might"
2852	"      have been within a few yards of us. But at last we could see"
2852	"      whence it came, and then we knew that we were indeed very close."
2852	"      A guttering candle was stuck in a crevice of the rocks which"
2852	"      flanked it on each side so as to keep the wind from it and also"
2852	"      to prevent it from being visible, save in the direction of"
2852	"      Baskerville Hall. A boulder of granite concealed our approach,"
2852	"      and crouching behind it we gazed over it at the signal light. It"
2852	"      was strange to see this single candle burning there in the middle"
2852	"      of the moor, with no sign of life near it—just the one straight"
2852	"      yellow flame and the gleam of the rock on each side of it."
2852	""
2852	"      “What shall we do now?” whispered Sir Henry."
2852	""
2852	"      “Wait here. He must be near his light. Let us see if we can get a"
2852	"      glimpse of him.”"
2852	""
2852	"      The words were hardly out of my mouth when we both saw him. Over"
2852	"      the rocks, in the crevice of which the candle burned, there was"
2852	"      thrust out an evil yellow face, a terrible animal face, all"
2852	"      seamed and scored with vile passions. Foul with mire, with a"
2852	"      bristling beard, and hung with matted hair, it might well have"
2852	"      belonged to one of those old savages who dwelt in the burrows on"
2852	"      the hillsides. The light beneath him was reflected in his small,"
2852	"      cunning eyes which peered fiercely to right and left through the"
2852	"      darkness like a crafty and savage animal who has heard the steps"
2852	"      of the hunters."
2852	""
2852	"      Something had evidently aroused his suspicions. It may have been"
2852	"      that Barrymore had some private signal which we had neglected to"
2852	"      give, or the fellow may have had some other reason for thinking"
2852	"      that all was not well, but I could read his fears upon his wicked"
2852	"      face. Any instant he might dash out the light and vanish in the"
2852	"      darkness. I sprang forward therefore, and Sir Henry did the same."
2852	"      At the same moment the convict screamed out a curse at us and"
2852	"      hurled a rock which splintered up against the boulder which had"
2852	"      sheltered us. I caught one glimpse of his short, squat, strongly"
2852	"      built figure as he sprang to his feet and turned to run. At the"
2852	"      same moment by a lucky chance the moon broke through the clouds."
2852	"      We rushed over the brow of the hill, and there was our man"
2852	"      running with great speed down the other side, springing over the"
2852	"      stones in his way with the activity of a mountain goat. A lucky"
2852	"      long shot of my revolver might have crippled him, but I had"
2852	"      brought it only to defend myself if attacked and not to shoot an"
2852	"      unarmed man who was running away."
2852	""
2852	"      We were both swift runners and in fairly good training, but we"
2852	"      soon found that we had no chance of overtaking him. We saw him"
2852	"      for a long time in the moonlight until he was only a small speck"
2852	"      moving swiftly among the boulders upon the side of a distant"
2852	"      hill. We ran and ran until we were completely blown, but the"
2852	"      space between us grew ever wider. Finally we stopped and sat"
2852	"      panting on two rocks, while we watched him disappearing in the"
2852	"      distance."
2852	""
2852	"      And it was at this moment that there occurred a most strange and"
2852	"      unexpected thing. We had risen from our rocks and were turning to"
2852	"      go home, having abandoned the hopeless chase. The moon was low"
2852	"      upon the right, and the jagged pinnacle of a granite tor stood up"
2852	"      against the lower curve of its silver disc. There, outlined as"
2852	"      black as an ebony statue on that shining background, I saw the"
2852	"      figure of a man upon the tor. Do not think that it was a"
2852	"      delusion, Holmes. I assure you that I have never in my life seen"
2852	"      anything more clearly. As far as I could judge, the figure was"
2852	"      that of a tall, thin man. He stood with his legs a little"
2852	"      separated, his arms folded, his head bowed, as if he were"
2852	"      brooding over that enormous wilderness of peat and granite which"
2852	"      lay before him. He might have been the very spirit of that"
2852	"      terrible place. It was not the convict. This man was far from the"
2852	"      place where the latter had disappeared. Besides, he was a much"
2852	"      taller man. With a cry of surprise I pointed him out to the"
2852	"      baronet, but in the instant during which I had turned to grasp"
2852	"      his arm the man was gone. There was the sharp pinnacle of granite"
2852	"      still cutting the lower edge of the moon, but its peak bore no"
2852	"      trace of that silent and motionless figure."
2852	""
2852	"      I wished to go in that direction and to search the tor, but it"
2852	"      was some distance away. The baronet’s nerves were still quivering"
2852	"      from that cry, which recalled the dark story of his family, and"
2852	"      he was not in the mood for fresh adventures. He had not seen this"
2852	"      lonely man upon the tor and could not feel the thrill which his"
2852	"      strange presence and his commanding attitude had given to me. “A"
2852	"      warder, no doubt,” said he. “The moor has been thick with them"
2852	"      since this fellow escaped.” Well, perhaps his explanation may be"
2852	"      the right one, but I should like to have some further proof of"
2852	"      it. Today we mean to communicate to the Princetown people where"
2852	"      they should look for their missing man, but it is hard lines that"
2852	"      we have not actually had the triumph of bringing him back as our"
2852	"      own prisoner. Such are the adventures of last night, and you must"
2852	"      acknowledge, my dear Holmes, that I have done you very well in"
2852	"      the matter of a report. Much of what I tell you is no doubt quite"
2852	"      irrelevant, but still I feel that it is best that I should let"
2852	"      you have all the facts and leave you to select for yourself those"
2852	"      which will be of most service to you in helping you to your"
2852	"      conclusions. We are certainly making some progress. So far as the"
2852	"      Barrymores go we have found the motive of their actions, and that"
2852	"      has cleared up the situation very much. But the moor with its"
2852	"      mysteries and its strange inhabitants remains as inscrutable as"
2852	"      ever. Perhaps in my next I may be able to throw some light upon"
2852	"      this also. Best of all would it be if you could come down to us."
2852	"      In any case you will hear from me again in the course of the next"
2852	"      few days."
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"<Chapter 10>"
2852	"Extract from the Diary of Dr. Watson"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"      So far I have been able to quote from the reports which I have"
2852	"      forwarded during these early days to Sherlock Holmes. Now,"
2852	"      however, I have arrived at a point in my narrative where I am"
2852	"      compelled to abandon this method and to trust once more to my"
2852	"      recollections, aided by the diary which I kept at the time. A few"
2852	"      extracts from the latter will carry me on to those scenes which"
2852	"      are indelibly fixed in every detail upon my memory. I proceed,"
2852	"      then, from the morning which followed our abortive chase of the"
2852	"      convict and our other strange experiences upon the moor."
2852	""
2852	"      _October_ 16_th_.—A dull and foggy day with a drizzle of rain."
2852	"      The house is banked in with rolling clouds, which rise now and"
2852	"      then to show the dreary curves of the moor, with thin, silver"
2852	"      veins upon the sides of the hills, and the distant boulders"
2852	"      gleaming where the light strikes upon their wet faces. It is"
2852	"      melancholy outside and in. The baronet is in a black reaction"
2852	"      after the excitements of the night. I am conscious myself of a"
2852	"      weight at my heart and a feeling of impending danger—ever present"
2852	"      danger, which is the more terrible because I am unable to define"
2852	"      it."
2852	""
2852	"      And have I not cause for such a feeling? Consider the long"
2852	"      sequence of incidents which have all pointed to some sinister"
2852	"      influence which is at work around us. There is the death of the"
2852	"      last occupant of the Hall, fulfilling so exactly the conditions"
2852	"      of the family legend, and there are the repeated reports from"
2852	"      peasants of the appearance of a strange creature upon the moor."
2852	"      Twice I have with my own ears heard the sound which resembled the"
2852	"      distant baying of a hound. It is incredible, impossible, that it"
2852	"      should really be outside the ordinary laws of nature. A spectral"
2852	"      hound which leaves material footmarks and fills the air with its"
2852	"      howling is surely not to be thought of. Stapleton may fall in"
2852	"      with such a superstition, and Mortimer also, but if I have one"
2852	"      quality upon earth it is common sense, and nothing will persuade"
2852	"      me to believe in such a thing. To do so would be to descend to"
2852	"      the level of these poor peasants, who are not content with a mere"
2852	"      fiend dog but must needs describe him with hell-fire shooting"
2852	"      from his mouth and eyes. Holmes would not listen to such fancies,"
2852	"      and I am his agent. But facts are facts, and I have twice heard"
2852	"      this crying upon the moor. Suppose that there were really some"
2852	"      huge hound loose upon it; that would go far to explain"
2852	"      everything. But where could such a hound lie concealed, where did"
2852	"      it get its food, where did it come from, how was it that no one"
2852	"      saw it by day? It must be confessed that the natural explanation"
2852	"      offers almost as many difficulties as the other. And always,"
2852	"      apart from the hound, there is the fact of the human agency in"
2852	"      London, the man in the cab, and the letter which warned Sir Henry"
2852	"      against the moor. This at least was real, but it might have been"
2852	"      the work of a protecting friend as easily as of an enemy. Where"
2852	"      is that friend or enemy now? Has he remained in London, or has he"
2852	"      followed us down here? Could he—could he be the stranger whom I"
2852	"      saw upon the tor?"
2852	""
2852	"      It is true that I have had only the one glance at him, and yet"
2852	"      there are some things to which I am ready to swear. He is no one"
2852	"      whom I have seen down here, and I have now met all the"
2852	"      neighbours. The figure was far taller than that of Stapleton, far"
2852	"      thinner than that of Frankland. Barrymore it might possibly have"
2852	"      been, but we had left him behind us, and I am certain that he"
2852	"      could not have followed us. A stranger then is still dogging us,"
2852	"      just as a stranger dogged us in London. We have never shaken him"
2852	"      off. If I could lay my hands upon that man, then at last we might"
2852	"      find ourselves at the end of all our difficulties. To this one"
2852	"      purpose I must now devote all my energies."
2852	""
2852	"      My first impulse was to tell Sir Henry all my plans. My second"
2852	"      and wisest one is to play my own game and speak as little as"
2852	"      possible to anyone. He is silent and distrait. His nerves have"
2852	"      been strangely shaken by that sound upon the moor. I will say"
2852	"      nothing to add to his anxieties, but I will take my own steps to"
2852	"      attain my own end."
2852	""
2852	"      We had a small scene this morning after breakfast. Barrymore"
2852	"      asked leave to speak with Sir Henry, and they were closeted in"
2852	"      his study some little time. Sitting in the billiard-room I more"
2852	"      than once heard the sound of voices raised, and I had a pretty"
2852	"      good idea what the point was which was under discussion. After a"
2852	"      time the baronet opened his door and called for me. “Barrymore"
2852	"      considers that he has a grievance,” he said. “He thinks that it"
2852	"      was unfair on our part to hunt his brother-in-law down when he,"
2852	"      of his own free will, had told us the secret.”"
2852	""
2852	"      The butler was standing very pale but very collected before us."
2852	""
2852	"      “I may have spoken too warmly, sir,” said he, “and if I have, I"
2852	"      am sure that I beg your pardon. At the same time, I was very much"
2852	"      surprised when I heard you two gentlemen come back this morning"
2852	"      and learned that you had been chasing Selden. The poor fellow has"
2852	"      enough to fight against without my putting more upon his track.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “If you had told us of your own free will it would have been a"
2852	"      different thing,” said the baronet, “you only told us, or rather"
2852	"      your wife only told us, when it was forced from you and you could"
2852	"      not help yourself.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I didn’t think you would have taken advantage of it, Sir"
2852	"      Henry—indeed I didn’t.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “The man is a public danger. There are lonely houses scattered"
2852	"      over the moor, and he is a fellow who would stick at nothing. You"
2852	"      only want to get a glimpse of his face to see that. Look at Mr."
2852	"      Stapleton’s house, for example, with no one but himself to defend"
2852	"      it. There’s no safety for anyone until he is under lock and key.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “He’ll break into no house, sir. I give you my solemn word upon"
2852	"      that. But he will never trouble anyone in this country again. I"
2852	"      assure you, Sir Henry, that in a very few days the necessary"
2852	"      arrangements will have been made and he will be on his way to"
2852	"      South America. For God’s sake, sir, I beg of you not to let the"
2852	"      police know that he is still on the moor. They have given up the"
2852	"      chase there, and he can lie quiet until the ship is ready for"
2852	"      him. You can’t tell on him without getting my wife and me into"
2852	"      trouble. I beg you, sir, to say nothing to the police.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “What do you say, Watson?”"
2852	""
2852	"      I shrugged my shoulders. “If he were safely out of the country it"
2852	"      would relieve the tax-payer of a burden.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But how about the chance of his holding someone up before he"
2852	"      goes?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “He would not do anything so mad, sir. We have provided him with"
2852	"      all that he can want. To commit a crime would be to show where he"
2852	"      was hiding.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “That is true,” said Sir Henry. “Well, Barrymore—”"
2852	""
2852	"      “God bless you, sir, and thank you from my heart! It would have"
2852	"      killed my poor wife had he been taken again.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I guess we are aiding and abetting a felony, Watson? But, after"
2852	"      what we have heard I don’t feel as if I could give the man up, so"
2852	"      there is an end of it. All right, Barrymore, you can go.”"
2852	""
2852	"      With a few broken words of gratitude the man turned, but he"
2852	"      hesitated and then came back."
2852	""
2852	"      “You’ve been so kind to us, sir, that I should like to do the"
2852	"      best I can for you in return. I know something, Sir Henry, and"
2852	"      perhaps I should have said it before, but it was long after the"
2852	"      inquest that I found it out. I’ve never breathed a word about it"
2852	"      yet to mortal man. It’s about poor Sir Charles’s death.”"
2852	""
2852	"      The baronet and I were both upon our feet. “Do you know how he"
2852	"      died?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, sir, I don’t know that.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “What then?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I know why he was at the gate at that hour. It was to meet a"
2852	"      woman.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “To meet a woman! He?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, sir.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “And the woman’s name?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I can’t give you the name, sir, but I can give you the initials."
2852	"      Her initials were L. L.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “How do you know this, Barrymore?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, Sir Henry, your uncle had a letter that morning. He had"
2852	"      usually a great many letters, for he was a public man and well"
2852	"      known for his kind heart, so that everyone who was in trouble was"
2852	"      glad to turn to him. But that morning, as it chanced, there was"
2852	"      only this one letter, so I took the more notice of it. It was"
2852	"      from Coombe Tracey, and it was addressed in a woman’s hand.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Well?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, sir, I thought no more of the matter, and never would have"
2852	"      done had it not been for my wife. Only a few weeks ago she was"
2852	"      cleaning out Sir Charles’s study—it had never been touched since"
2852	"      his death—and she found the ashes of a burned letter in the back"
2852	"      of the grate. The greater part of it was charred to pieces, but"
2852	"      one little slip, the end of a page, hung together, and the"
2852	"      writing could still be read, though it was grey on a black"
2852	"      ground. It seemed to us to be a postscript at the end of the"
2852	"      letter and it said: ‘Please, please, as you are a gentleman, burn"
2852	"      this letter, and be at the gate by ten o clock. Beneath it were"
2852	"      signed the initials L. L.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Have you got that slip?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, sir, it crumbled all to bits after we moved it.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Had Sir Charles received any other letters in the same writing?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, sir, I took no particular notice of his letters. I should"
2852	"      not have noticed this one, only it happened to come alone.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “And you have no idea who L. L. is?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, sir. No more than you have. But I expect if we could lay our"
2852	"      hands upon that lady we should know more about Sir Charles’s"
2852	"      death.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I cannot understand, Barrymore, how you came to conceal this"
2852	"      important information.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, sir, it was immediately after that our own trouble came to"
2852	"      us. And then again, sir, we were both of us very fond of Sir"
2852	"      Charles, as we well might be considering all that he has done for"
2852	"      us. To rake this up couldn’t help our poor master, and it’s well"
2852	"      to go carefully when there’s a lady in the case. Even the best of"
2852	"      us—”"
2852	""
2852	"      “You thought it might injure his reputation?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, sir, I thought no good could come of it. But now you have"
2852	"      been kind to us, and I feel as if it would be treating you"
2852	"      unfairly not to tell you all that I know about the matter.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Very good, Barrymore; you can go.” When the butler had left us"
2852	"      Sir Henry turned to me. “Well, Watson, what do you think of this"
2852	"      new light?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “It seems to leave the darkness rather blacker than before.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “So I think. But if we can only trace L. L. it should clear up"
2852	"      the whole business. We have gained that much. We know that there"
2852	"      is someone who has the facts if we can only find her. What do you"
2852	"      think we should do?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Let Holmes know all about it at once. It will give him the clue"
2852	"      for which he has been seeking. I am much mistaken if it does not"
2852	"      bring him down.”"
2852	""
2852	"      I went at once to my room and drew up my report of the morning’s"
2852	"      conversation for Holmes. It was evident to me that he had been"
2852	"      very busy of late, for the notes which I had from Baker Street"
2852	"      were few and short, with no comments upon the information which I"
2852	"      had supplied and hardly any reference to my mission. No doubt his"
2852	"      blackmailing case is absorbing all his faculties. And yet this"
2852	"      new factor must surely arrest his attention and renew his"
2852	"      interest. I wish that he were here."
2852	""
2852	"      _October_ 17_th_.—All day today the rain poured down, rustling on"
2852	"      the ivy and dripping from the eaves. I thought of the convict out"
2852	"      upon the bleak, cold, shelterless moor. Poor devil! Whatever his"
2852	"      crimes, he has suffered something to atone for them. And then I"
2852	"      thought of that other one—the face in the cab, the figure against"
2852	"      the moon. Was he also out in that deluged—the unseen watcher, the"
2852	"      man of darkness? In the evening I put on my waterproof and I"
2852	"      walked far upon the sodden moor, full of dark imaginings, the"
2852	"      rain beating upon my face and the wind whistling about my ears."
2852	"      God help those who wander into the great mire now, for even the"
2852	"      firm uplands are becoming a morass. I found the black tor upon"
2852	"      which I had seen the solitary watcher, and from its craggy summit"
2852	"      I looked out myself across the melancholy downs. Rain squalls"
2852	"      drifted across their russet face, and the heavy, slate-coloured"
2852	"      clouds hung low over the landscape, trailing in grey wreaths down"
2852	"      the sides of the fantastic hills. In the distant hollow on the"
2852	"      left, half hidden by the mist, the two thin towers of Baskerville"
2852	"      Hall rose above the trees. They were the only signs of human life"
2852	"      which I could see, save only those prehistoric huts which lay"
2852	"      thickly upon the slopes of the hills. Nowhere was there any trace"
2852	"      of that lonely man whom I had seen on the same spot two nights"
2852	"      before."
2852	""
2852	"      As I walked back I was overtaken by Dr. Mortimer driving in his"
2852	"      dog-cart over a rough moorland track which led from the outlying"
2852	"      farmhouse of Foulmire. He has been very attentive to us, and"
2852	"      hardly a day has passed that he has not called at the Hall to see"
2852	"      how we were getting on. He insisted upon my climbing into his"
2852	"      dog-cart, and he gave me a lift homeward. I found him much"
2852	"      troubled over the disappearance of his little spaniel. It had"
2852	"      wandered on to the moor and had never come back. I gave him such"
2852	"      consolation as I might, but I thought of the pony on the Grimpen"
2852	"      Mire, and I do not fancy that he will see his little dog again."
2852	""
2852	"      “By the way, Mortimer,” said I as we jolted along the rough road,"
2852	"      “I suppose there are few people living within driving distance of"
2852	"      this whom you do not know?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Hardly any, I think.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Can you, then, tell me the name of any woman whose initials are"
2852	"      L. L.?”"
2852	""
2852	"      He thought for a few minutes."
2852	""
2852	"      “No,” said he. “There are a few gipsies and labouring folk for"
2852	"      whom I can’t answer, but among the farmers or gentry there is no"
2852	"      one whose initials are those. Wait a bit though,” he added after"
2852	"      a pause. “There is Laura Lyons—her initials are L. L.—but she"
2852	"      lives in Coombe Tracey.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Who is she?” I asked."
2852	""
2852	"      “She is Frankland’s daughter.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “What! Old Frankland the crank?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Exactly. She married an artist named Lyons, who came sketching"
2852	"      on the moor. He proved to be a blackguard and deserted her. The"
2852	"      fault from what I hear may not have been entirely on one side."
2852	"      Her father refused to have anything to do with her because she"
2852	"      had married without his consent and perhaps for one or two other"
2852	"      reasons as well. So, between the old sinner and the young one the"
2852	"      girl has had a pretty bad time.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “How does she live?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I fancy old Frankland allows her a pittance, but it cannot be"
2852	"      more, for his own affairs are considerably involved. Whatever she"
2852	"      may have deserved one could not allow her to go hopelessly to the"
2852	"      bad. Her story got about, and several of the people here did"
2852	"      something to enable her to earn an honest living. Stapleton did"
2852	"      for one, and Sir Charles for another. I gave a trifle myself. It"
2852	"      was to set her up in a typewriting business.”"
2852	""
2852	"      He wanted to know the object of my inquiries, but I managed to"
2852	"      satisfy his curiosity without telling him too much, for there is"
2852	"      no reason why we should take anyone into our confidence. Tomorrow"
2852	"      morning I shall find my way to Coombe Tracey, and if I can see"
2852	"      this Mrs. Laura Lyons, of equivocal reputation, a long step will"
2852	"      have been made towards clearing one incident in this chain of"
2852	"      mysteries. I am certainly developing the wisdom of the serpent,"
2852	"      for when Mortimer pressed his questions to an inconvenient extent"
2852	"      I asked him casually to what type Frankland’s skull belonged, and"
2852	"      so heard nothing but craniology for the rest of our drive. I have"
2852	"      not lived for years with Sherlock Holmes for nothing."
2852	""
2852	"      I have only one other incident to record upon this tempestuous"
2852	"      and melancholy day. This was my conversation with Barrymore just"
2852	"      now, which gives me one more strong card which I can play in due"
2852	"      time."
2852	""
2852	"      Mortimer had stayed to dinner, and he and the baronet played"
2852	"      écarté afterwards. The butler brought me my coffee into the"
2852	"      library, and I took the chance to ask him a few questions."
2852	""
2852	"      “Well,” said I, “has this precious relation of yours departed, or"
2852	"      is he still lurking out yonder?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I don’t know, sir. I hope to heaven that he has gone, for he has"
2852	"      brought nothing but trouble here! I’ve not heard of him since I"
2852	"      left out food for him last, and that was three days ago.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Did you see him then?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, sir, but the food was gone when next I went that way.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Then he was certainly there?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “So you would think, sir, unless it was the other man who took"
2852	"      it.”"
2852	""

2852	"      Barrymore."
2852	""
2852	"      “You know that there is another man then?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, sir; there is another man upon the moor.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Have you seen him?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, sir.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “How do you know of him then?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Selden told me of him, sir, a week ago or more. He’s in hiding,"

2852	"      like it, Dr. Watson—I tell you straight, sir, that I don’t like"
2852	"      it.” He spoke with a sudden passion of earnestness."
2852	""
2852	"      “Now, listen to me, Barrymore! I have no interest in this matter"
2852	"      but that of your master. I have come here with no object except"
2852	"      to help him. Tell me, frankly, what it is that you don’t like.”"
2852	""
2852	"      Barrymore hesitated for a moment, as if he regretted his outburst"
2852	"      or found it difficult to express his own feelings in words."
2852	""
2852	"      “It’s all these goings-on, sir,” he cried at last, waving his"
2852	"      hand towards the rain-lashed window which faced the moor."
2852	"      “There’s foul play somewhere, and there’s black villainy brewing,"
2852	"      to that I’ll swear! Very glad I should be, sir, to see Sir Henry"
2852	"      on his way back to London again!”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But what is it that alarms you?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Look at Sir Charles’s death! That was bad enough, for all that"
2852	"      the coroner said. Look at the noises on the moor at night."
2852	"      There’s not a man would cross it after sundown if he was paid for"

2852	"      good to anyone of the name of Baskerville, and very glad I shall"
2852	"      be to be quit of it all on the day that Sir Henry’s new servants"
2852	"      are ready to take over the Hall.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But about this stranger,” said I. “Can you tell me anything"
2852	"      about him? What did Selden say? Did he find out where he hid, or"
2852	"      what he was doing?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “He saw him once or twice, but he is a deep one and gives nothing"
2852	"      away. At first he thought that he was the police, but soon he"
2852	"      found that he had some lay of his own. A kind of gentleman he"
2852	"      was, as far as he could see, but what he was doing he could not"
2852	"      make out.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “And where did he say that he lived?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Among the old houses on the hillside—the stone huts where the"
2852	"      old folk used to live.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But how about his food?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Selden found out that he has got a lad who works for him and"
2852	"      brings all he needs. I dare say he goes to Coombe Tracey for what"
2852	"      he wants.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Very good, Barrymore. We may talk further of this some other"
2852	"      time.” When the butler had gone I walked over to the black"
2852	"      window, and I looked through a blurred pane at the driving clouds"
2852	"      and at the tossing outline of the wind-swept trees. It is a wild"
2852	"      night indoors, and what must it be in a stone hut upon the moor."
2852	"      What passion of hatred can it be which leads a man to lurk in"
2852	"      such a place at such a time! And what deep and earnest purpose"
2852	"      can he have which calls for such a trial! There, in that hut upon"
2852	"      the moor, seems to lie the very centre of that problem which has"
2852	"      vexed me so sorely. I swear that another day shall not have"
2852	"      passed before I have done all that man can do to reach the heart"
2852	"      of the mystery."
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"<Chapter 11>"
2852	"The Man on the Tor"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"      The extract from my private diary which forms the last chapter"
2852	"      has brought my narrative up to the eighteenth of October, a time"
2852	"      when these strange events began to move swiftly towards their"
2852	"      terrible conclusion. The incidents of the next few days are"
2852	"      indelibly graven upon my recollection, and I can tell them"
2852	"      without reference to the notes made at the time. I start them"
2852	"      from the day which succeeded that upon which I had established"
2852	"      two facts of great importance, the one that Mrs. Laura Lyons of"
2852	"      Coombe Tracey had written to Sir Charles Baskerville and made an"
2852	"      appointment with him at the very place and hour that he met his"
2852	"      death, the other that the lurking man upon the moor was to be"
2852	"      found among the stone huts upon the hillside. With these two"
2852	"      facts in my possession I felt that either my intelligence or my"
2852	"      courage must be deficient if I could not throw some further light"
2852	"      upon these dark places."
2852	""
2852	"      I had no opportunity to tell the baronet what I had learned about"
2852	"      Mrs. Lyons upon the evening before, for Dr. Mortimer remained"
2852	"      with him at cards until it was very late. At breakfast, however,"
2852	"      I informed him about my discovery and asked him whether he would"
2852	"      care to accompany me to Coombe Tracey. At first he was very eager"
2852	"      to come, but on second thoughts it seemed to both of us that if I"
2852	"      went alone the results might be better. The more formal we made"
2852	"      the visit the less information we might obtain. I left Sir Henry"
2852	"      behind, therefore, not without some prickings of conscience, and"
2852	"      drove off upon my new quest."
2852	""
2852	"      When I reached Coombe Tracey I told Perkins to put up the horses,"
2852	"      and I made inquiries for the lady whom I had come to interrogate."
2852	"      I had no difficulty in finding her rooms, which were central and"
2852	"      well appointed. A maid showed me in without ceremony, and as I"
2852	"      entered the sitting-room a lady, who was sitting before a"
2852	"      Remington typewriter, sprang up with a pleasant smile of welcome."
2852	"      Her face fell, however, when she saw that I was a stranger, and"
2852	"      she sat down again and asked me the object of my visit."
2852	""
2852	"      The first impression left by Mrs. Lyons was one of extreme"
2852	"      beauty. Her eyes and hair were of the same rich hazel colour, and"
2852	"      her cheeks, though considerably freckled, were flushed with the"
2852	"      exquisite bloom of the brunette, the dainty pink which lurks at"
2852	"      the heart of the sulphur rose. Admiration was, I repeat, the"
2852	"      first impression. But the second was criticism. There was"
2852	"      something subtly wrong with the face, some coarseness of"
2852	"      expression, some hardness, perhaps, of eye, some looseness of lip"
2852	"      which marred its perfect beauty. But these, of course, are"
2852	"      afterthoughts. At the moment I was simply conscious that I was in"
2852	"      the presence of a very handsome woman, and that she was asking me"
2852	"      the reasons for my visit. I had not quite understood until that"
2852	"      instant how delicate my mission was."
2852	""
2852	"      “I have the pleasure,” said I, “of knowing your father.”"
2852	""
2852	"      It was a clumsy introduction, and the lady made me feel it."
2852	"      “There is nothing in common between my father and me,” she said."
2852	"      “I owe him nothing, and his friends are not mine. If it were not"
2852	"      for the late Sir Charles Baskerville and some other kind hearts I"
2852	"      might have starved for all that my father cared.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “It was about the late Sir Charles Baskerville that I have come"
2852	"      here to see you.”"
2852	""
2852	"      The freckles started out on the lady’s face."
2852	""
2852	"      “What can I tell you about him?” she asked, and her fingers"
2852	"      played nervously over the stops of her typewriter."
2852	""
2852	"      “You knew him, did you not?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I have already said that I owe a great deal to his kindness. If"
2852	"      I am able to support myself it is largely due to the interest"
2852	"      which he took in my unhappy situation.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Did you correspond with him?”"
2852	""
2852	"      The lady looked quickly up with an angry gleam in her hazel eyes."
2852	""
2852	"      “What is the object of these questions?” she asked sharply."
2852	""
2852	"      “The object is to avoid a public scandal. It is better that I"
2852	"      should ask them here than that the matter should pass outside our"
2852	"      control.”"
2852	""
2852	"      She was silent and her face was still very pale. At last she"
2852	"      looked up with something reckless and defiant in her manner."
2852	""
2852	"      “Well, I’ll answer,” she said. “What are your questions?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Did you correspond with Sir Charles?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I certainly wrote to him once or twice to acknowledge his"
2852	"      delicacy and his generosity.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Have you the dates of those letters?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Have you ever met him?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, once or twice, when he came into Coombe Tracey. He was a"
2852	"      very retiring man, and he preferred to do good by stealth.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “But if you saw him so seldom and wrote so seldom, how did he"
2852	"      know enough about your affairs to be able to help you, as you say"

2852	""

2852	""

2852	""
2852	"      I knew already that Sir Charles Baskerville had made Stapleton"

2852	""
2852	"      “Then I answer, certainly not.”"

2852	""
2852	"      The flush had faded in an instant, and a deathly face was before"

2852	""
2852	"      “I never went.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Mrs. Lyons!”"
2852	""

2852	"      Something intervened to prevent my going.”"
2852	""

2852	""

2852	""

2852	""
2852	"      “I have heard so much.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “My life has been one incessant persecution from a husband whom I"

2852	"      The woman’s story hung coherently together, and all my questions"
2852	"      were unable to shake it. I could only check it by finding if she"
2852	"      had, indeed, instituted divorce proceedings against her husband"
2852	"      at or about the time of the tragedy."
2852	""
2852	"      It was unlikely that she would dare to say that she had not been"
2852	"      to Baskerville Hall if she really had been, for a trap would be"
2852	"      necessary to take her there, and could not have returned to"
2852	"      Coombe Tracey until the early hours of the morning. Such an"
2852	"      excursion could not be kept secret. The probability was,"
2852	"      therefore, that she was telling the truth, or, at least, a part"

2852	"      had reached that dead wall which seemed to be built across every"

2852	"      could proceed no farther in that direction, but must turn back to"
2852	"      that other clue which was to be sought for among the stone huts"
2852	"      upon the moor."
2852	""
2852	"      And that was a most vague direction. I realised it as I drove"
2852	"      back and noted how hill after hill showed traces of the ancient"
2852	"      people. Barrymore’s only indication had been that the stranger"
2852	"      lived in one of these abandoned huts, and many hundreds of them"
2852	"      are scattered throughout the length and breadth of the moor. But"
2852	"      I had my own experience for a guide since it had shown me the man"
2852	"      himself standing upon the summit of the Black Tor. That, then,"
2852	"      should be the centre of my search. From there I should explore"
2852	"      every hut upon the moor until I lighted upon the right one. If"
2852	"      this man were inside it I should find out from his own lips, at"
2852	"      the point of my revolver if necessary, who he was and why he had"
2852	"      dogged us so long. He might slip away from us in the crowd of"

2852	"      moor. On the other hand, if I should find the hut and its tenant"
2852	"      should not be within it I must remain there, however long the"
2852	"      vigil, until he returned. Holmes had missed him in London. It"
2852	"      would indeed be a triumph for me if I could run him to earth"

2852	""
2852	"      Luck had been against us again and again in this inquiry, but now"
2852	"      at last it came to my aid. And the messenger of good fortune was"
2852	"      none other than Mr. Frankland, who was standing, grey-whiskered"
2852	"      and red-faced, outside the gate of his garden, which opened on to"
2852	"      the highroad along which I travelled."
2852	""
2852	"      “Good-day, Dr. Watson,” cried he with unwonted good humour, “you"
2852	"      must really give your horses a rest and come in to have a glass"

2852	""
2852	"      “It is a great day for me, sir—one of the red-letter days of my"
2852	"      life,” he cried with many chuckles. “I have brought off a double"
2852	"      event. I mean to teach them in these parts that law is law, and"
2852	"      that there is a man here who does not fear to invoke it. I have"
2852	"      established a right of way through the centre of old Middleton’s"
2852	"      park, slap across it, sir, within a hundred yards of his own"

2852	"      that they cannot ride roughshod over the rights of the commoners,"

2852	"      used to picnic. These infernal people seem to think that there"

2852	"      Watson, and both in my favour. I haven’t had such a day since I"
2852	"      had Sir John Morland for trespass because he shot in his own"
2852	"      warren.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “How on earth did you do that?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Look it up in the books, sir. It will repay reading—Frankland"
2852	"      _v_. Morland, Court of Queen’s Bench. It cost me £200, but I got"
2852	"      my verdict.”"

2852	"      “Did it do you any good?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “None, sir, none. I am proud to say that I had no interest in the"

2852	"      doubt, for example, that the Fernworthy people will burn me in"
2852	"      effigy tonight. I told the police last time they did it that they"
2852	"      should stop these disgraceful exhibitions. The County"
2852	"      Constabulary is in a scandalous state, sir, and it has not"
2852	"      afforded me the protection to which I am entitled. The case of"
2852	"      Frankland _v_. Regina will bring the matter before the attention"
2852	"      of the public. I told them that they would have occasion to"
2852	"      regret their treatment of me, and already my words have come"
2852	"      true.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “How so?” I asked."
2852	""
