2852	"<Chapter 13>"
2852	"Fixing the Nets"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"      Sir Henry was more pleased than surprised to see Sherlock Holmes,"
2852	"      for he had for some days been expecting that recent events would"
2852	"      bring him down from London. He did raise his eyebrows, however,"
2852	"      when he found that my friend had neither any luggage nor any"
2852	"      explanations for its absence. Between us we soon supplied his"
2852	"      wants, and then over a belated supper we explained to the baronet"
2852	"      as much of our experience as it seemed desirable that he should"
2852	"      know. But first I had the unpleasant duty of breaking the news to"
2852	"      Barrymore and his wife. To him it may have been an unmitigated"
2852	"      relief, but she wept bitterly in her apron. To all the world he"
2852	"      was the man of violence, half animal and half demon; but to her"
2852	"      he always remained the little wilful boy of her own girlhood, the"
2852	"      child who had clung to her hand. Evil indeed is the man who has"
2852	"      not one woman to mourn him."
2852	""
2852	"      “I’ve been moping in the house all day since Watson went off in"

2852	"      about alone I might have had a more lively evening, for I had a"

2852	"      statue, a personification of alertness and expectation."

2852	""
2852	"<Chapter 14>"
2852	"The Hound of the Baskervilles"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"      One of Sherlock Holmes’s defects—if, indeed, one may call it a"
2852	"      defect—was that he was exceedingly loath to communicate his full"
2852	"      plans to any other person until the instant of their fulfilment."
2852	"      Partly it came no doubt from his own masterful nature, which"
2852	"      loved to dominate and surprise those who were around him. Partly"
2852	"      also from his professional caution, which urged him never to take"
2852	"      any chances. The result, however, was very trying for those who"
2852	"      were acting as his agents and assistants. I had often suffered"
2852	"      under it, but never more so than during that long drive in the"
2852	"      darkness. The great ordeal was in front of us; at last we were"
2852	"      about to make our final effort, and yet Holmes had said nothing,"
2852	"      and I could only surmise what his course of action would be. My"
2852	"      nerves thrilled with anticipation when at last the cold wind upon"
2852	"      our faces and the dark, void spaces on either side of the narrow"
2852	"      road told me that we were back upon the moor once again. Every"
2852	"      stride of the horses and every turn of the wheels was taking us"
2852	"      nearer to our supreme adventure."
2852	""
2852	"      Our conversation was hampered by the presence of the driver of"
2852	"      the hired wagonette, so that we were forced to talk of trivial"
2852	"      matters when our nerves were tense with emotion and anticipation."
2852	"      It was a relief to me, after that unnatural restraint, when we at"
2852	"      last passed Frankland’s house and knew that we were drawing near"
2852	"      to the Hall and to the scene of action. We did not drive up to"
2852	"      the door but got down near the gate of the avenue. The wagonette"
2852	"      was paid off and ordered to return to Coombe Tracey forthwith,"
2852	"      while we started to walk to Merripit House."
2852	""
2852	"      “Are you armed, Lestrade?”"
2852	""
2852	"      The little detective smiled. “As long as I have my trousers I"
2852	"      have a hip-pocket, and as long as I have my hip-pocket I have"
2852	"      something in it.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Good! My friend and I are also ready for emergencies.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “You’re mighty close about this affair, Mr. Holmes. What’s the"
2852	"      game now?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “A waiting game.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “My word, it does not seem a very cheerful place,” said the"
2852	"      detective with a shiver, glancing round him at the gloomy slopes"
2852	"      of the hill and at the huge lake of fog which lay over the"
2852	"      Grimpen Mire. “I see the lights of a house ahead of us.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “That is Merripit House and the end of our journey. I must"
2852	"      request you to walk on tiptoe and not to talk above a whisper.”"
2852	""

2852	"      house, but Holmes halted us when we were about two hundred yards"
2852	"      from it."
2852	""
2852	"      “This will do,” said he. “These rocks upon the right make an"
2852	"      admirable screen.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “We are to wait here?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, we shall make our little ambush here. Get into this hollow,"
2852	"      Lestrade. You have been inside the house, have you not, Watson?"
2852	"      Can you tell the position of the rooms? What are those latticed"
2852	"      windows at this end?”"
2852	""

2852	""
2852	"      “And the one beyond, which shines so brightly?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “That is certainly the dining-room.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “The blinds are up. You know the lie of the land best. Creep"
2852	"      forward quietly and see what they are doing—but for heaven’s sake"
2852	"      don’t let them know that they are watched!”"
2852	""
2852	"      I tiptoed down the path and stooped behind the low wall which"
2852	"      surrounded the stunted orchard. Creeping in its shadow I reached"
2852	"      a point whence I could look straight through the uncurtained"
2852	"      window."
2852	""
2852	"      There were only two men in the room, Sir Henry and Stapleton."
2852	"      They sat with their profiles towards me on either side of the"
2852	"      round table. Both of them were smoking cigars, and coffee and"
2852	"      wine were in front of them. Stapleton was talking with animation,"
2852	"      but the baronet looked pale and distrait. Perhaps the thought of"
2852	"      that lonely walk across the ill-omened moor was weighing heavily"

2852	""

2852	"      Henry filled his glass again and leaned back in his chair,"
2852	"      puffing at his cigar. I heard the creak of a door and the crisp"
2852	"      sound of boots upon gravel. The steps passed along the path on"
2852	"      the other side of the wall under which I crouched. Looking over,"
2852	"      I saw the naturalist pause at the door of an out-house in the"
2852	"      corner of the orchard. A key turned in a lock, and as he passed"
2852	"      in there was a curious scuffling noise from within. He was only a"
2852	"      minute or so inside, and then I heard the key turn once more and"
2852	"      he passed me and reentered the house. I saw him rejoin his guest,"
2852	"      and I crept quietly back to where my companions were waiting to"
2852	"      tell them what I had seen."
2852	""
2852	"      “You say, Watson, that the lady is not there?” Holmes asked when"
2852	"      I had finished my report."
2852	""
2852	"      “No.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Where can she be, then, since there is no light in any other"
2852	"      room except the kitchen?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I cannot think where she is.”"
2852	""
2852	"      I have said that over the great Grimpen Mire there hung a dense,"
2852	"      white fog. It was drifting slowly in our direction and banked"
2852	"      itself up like a wall on that side of us, low but thick and well"
2852	"      defined. The moon shone on it, and it looked like a great"
2852	"      shimmering ice-field, with the heads of the distant tors as rocks"
2852	"      borne upon its surface. Holmes’s face was turned towards it, and"
2852	"      he muttered impatiently as he watched its sluggish drift."
2852	""
2852	"      “It’s moving towards us, Watson.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Is that serious?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Very serious, indeed—the one thing upon earth which could have"
2852	"      disarranged my plans. He can’t be very long, now. It is already"
2852	"      ten o’clock. Our success and even his life may depend upon his"
2852	"      coming out before the fog is over the path.”"
2852	""
2852	"      The night was clear and fine above us. The stars shone cold and"
2852	"      bright, while a half-moon bathed the whole scene in a soft,"
2852	"      uncertain light. Before us lay the dark bulk of the house, its"
2852	"      serrated roof and bristling chimneys hard outlined against the"
2852	"      silver-spangled sky. Broad bars of golden light from the lower"
2852	"      windows stretched across the orchard and the moor. One of them"
2852	"      was suddenly shut off. The servants had left the kitchen. There"
2852	"      only remained the lamp in the dining-room where the two men, the"
2852	"      murderous host and the unconscious guest, still chatted over"
2852	"      their cigars."
2852	""
2852	"      Every minute that white woolly plain which covered one-half of"
2852	"      the moor was drifting closer and closer to the house. Already the"
2852	"      first thin wisps of it were curling across the golden square of"
2852	"      the lighted window. The farther wall of the orchard was already"
2852	"      invisible, and the trees were standing out of a swirl of white"
2852	"      vapour. As we watched it the fog-wreaths came crawling round both"
2852	"      corners of the house and rolled slowly into one dense bank on"
2852	"      which the upper floor and the roof floated like a strange ship"
2852	"      upon a shadowy sea. Holmes struck his hand passionately upon the"
2852	"      rock in front of us and stamped his feet in his impatience."
2852	""
2852	"      “If he isn’t out in a quarter of an hour the path will be"
2852	"      covered. In half an hour we won’t be able to see our hands in"
2852	"      front of us.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Shall we move farther back upon higher ground?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes, I think it would be as well.”"
2852	""
2852	"      So as the fog-bank flowed onward we fell back before it until we"
2852	"      were half a mile from the house, and still that dense white sea,"
2852	"      with the moon silvering its upper edge, swept slowly and"
2852	"      inexorably on."
2852	""
2852	"      “We are going too far,” said Holmes. “We dare not take the chance"
2852	"      of his being overtaken before he can reach us. At all costs we"
2852	"      must hold our ground where we are.” He dropped on his knees and"
2852	"      clapped his ear to the ground. “Thank God, I think that I hear"
2852	"      him coming.”"
2852	""
2852	"      A sound of quick steps broke the silence of the moor. Crouching"
2852	"      among the stones we stared intently at the silver-tipped bank in"
2852	"      front of us. The steps grew louder, and through the fog, as"
2852	"      through a curtain, there stepped the man whom we were awaiting."
2852	"      He looked round him in surprise as he emerged into the clear,"
2852	"      starlit night. Then he came swiftly along the path, passed close"
2852	"      to where we lay, and went on up the long slope behind us. As he"
2852	"      walked he glanced continually over either shoulder, like a man"
2852	"      who is ill at ease."
2852	""
2852	"      “Hist!” cried Holmes, and I heard the sharp click of a cocking"
2852	"      pistol. “Look out! It’s coming!”"
2852	""
2852	"      There was a thin, crisp, continuous patter from somewhere in the"
2852	"      heart of that crawling bank. The cloud was within fifty yards of"
2852	"      where we lay, and we glared at it, all three, uncertain what"
2852	"      horror was about to break from the heart of it. I was at Holmes’s"
2852	"      elbow, and I glanced for an instant at his face. It was pale and"
2852	"      exultant, his eyes shining brightly in the moonlight. But"
2852	"      suddenly they started forward in a rigid, fixed stare, and his"
2852	"      lips parted in amazement. At the same instant Lestrade gave a"
2852	"      yell of terror and threw himself face downward upon the ground. I"
2852	"      sprang to my feet, my inert hand grasping my pistol, my mind"
2852	"      paralyzed by the dreadful shape which had sprung out upon us from"
2852	"      the shadows of the fog. A hound it was, an enormous coal-black"
2852	"      hound, but not such a hound as mortal eyes have ever seen. Fire"
2852	"      burst from its open mouth, its eyes glowed with a smouldering"
2852	"      glare, its muzzle and hackles and dewlap were outlined in"
2852	"      flickering flame. Never in the delirious dream of a disordered"
2852	"      brain could anything more savage, more appalling, more hellish be"
2852	"      conceived than that dark form and savage face which broke upon us"
2852	"      out of the wall of fog."
2852	""
2852	"      With long bounds the huge black creature was leaping down the"
2852	"      track, following hard upon the footsteps of our friend. So"
2852	"      paralyzed were we by the apparition that we allowed him to pass"
2852	"      before we had recovered our nerve. Then Holmes and I both fired"
2852	"      together, and the creature gave a hideous howl, which showed that"
2852	"      one at least had hit him. He did not pause, however, but bounded"
2852	"      onward. Far away on the path we saw Sir Henry looking back, his"
2852	"      face white in the moonlight, his hands raised in horror, glaring"
2852	"      helplessly at the frightful thing which was hunting him down. But"
2852	"      that cry of pain from the hound had blown all our fears to the"
2852	"      winds. If he was vulnerable he was mortal, and if we could wound"
2852	"      him we could kill him. Never have I seen a man run as Holmes ran"
2852	"      that night. I am reckoned fleet of foot, but he outpaced me as"
2852	"      much as I outpaced the little professional. In front of us as we"
2852	"      flew up the track we heard scream after scream from Sir Henry and"
2852	"      the deep roar of the hound. I was in time to see the beast spring"
2852	"      upon its victim, hurl him to the ground, and worry at his throat."
2852	"      But the next instant Holmes had emptied five barrels of his"
2852	"      revolver into the creature’s flank. With a last howl of agony and"
2852	"      a vicious snap in the air, it rolled upon its back, four feet"
2852	"      pawing furiously, and then fell limp upon its side. I stooped,"
2852	"      panting, and pressed my pistol to the dreadful, shimmering head,"
2852	"      but it was useless to press the trigger. The giant hound was"
2852	"      dead."
2852	""
2852	"      Sir Henry lay insensible where he had fallen. We tore away his"
2852	"      collar, and Holmes breathed a prayer of gratitude when we saw"
2852	"      that there was no sign of a wound and that the rescue had been in"
2852	"      time. Already our friend’s eyelids shivered and he made a feeble"
2852	"      effort to move. Lestrade thrust his brandy-flask between the"
2852	"      baronet’s teeth, and two frightened eyes were looking up at us."
2852	""
2852	"      “My God!” he whispered. “What was it? What, in heaven’s name, was"
2852	"      it?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “It’s dead, whatever it is,” said Holmes. “We’ve laid the family"
2852	"      ghost once and forever.”"
2852	""
2852	"      In mere size and strength it was a terrible creature which was"
2852	"      lying stretched before us. It was not a pure bloodhound and it"
2852	"      was not a pure mastiff; but it appeared to be a combination of"
2852	"      the two—gaunt, savage, and as large as a small lioness. Even now"
2852	"      in the stillness of death, the huge jaws seemed to be dripping"
2852	"      with a bluish flame and the small, deep-set, cruel eyes were"
2852	"      ringed with fire. I placed my hand upon the glowing muzzle, and"
2852	"      as I held them up my own fingers smouldered and gleamed in the"
2852	"      darkness."
2852	""
2852	"      “Phosphorus,” I said."
2852	""
2852	"      “A cunning preparation of it,” said Holmes, sniffing at the dead"
2852	"      animal. “There is no smell which might have interfered with his"
2852	"      power of scent. We owe you a deep apology, Sir Henry, for having"
2852	"      exposed you to this fright. I was prepared for a hound, but not"
2852	"      for such a creature as this. And the fog gave us little time to"
2852	"      receive him.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “You have saved my life.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Having first endangered it. Are you strong enough to stand?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Give me another mouthful of that brandy and I shall be ready for"
2852	"      anything. So! Now, if you will help me up. What do you propose to"
2852	"      do?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “To leave you here. You are not fit for further adventures"
2852	"      tonight. If you will wait, one or other of us will go back with"
2852	"      you to the Hall.”"
2852	""
2852	"      He tried to stagger to his feet; but he was still ghastly pale"
2852	"      and trembling in every limb. We helped him to a rock, where he"
2852	"      sat shivering with his face buried in his hands."
2852	""
2852	"      “We must leave you now,” said Holmes. “The rest of our work must"
2852	"      be done, and every moment is of importance. We have our case, and"
2852	"      now we only want our man."
2852	""
2852	"      “It’s a thousand to one against our finding him at the house,” he"
2852	"      continued as we retraced our steps swiftly down the path. “Those"
2852	"      shots must have told him that the game was up.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “We were some distance off, and this fog may have deadened them.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “He followed the hound to call him off—of that you may be"
2852	"      certain. No, no, he’s gone by this time! But we’ll search the"
2852	"      house and make sure.”"
2852	""
2852	"      The front door was open, so we rushed in and hurried from room to"
2852	"      room to the amazement of a doddering old manservant, who met us"
2852	"      in the passage. There was no light save in the dining-room, but"
2852	"      Holmes caught up the lamp and left no corner of the house"
2852	"      unexplored. No sign could we see of the man whom we were chasing."
2852	"      On the upper floor, however, one of the bedroom doors was locked."
2852	""
2852	"      “There’s someone in here,” cried Lestrade. “I can hear a"
2852	"      movement. Open this door!”"
2852	""
2852	"      A faint moaning and rustling came from within. Holmes struck the"
2852	"      door just over the lock with the flat of his foot and it flew"
2852	"      open. Pistol in hand, we all three rushed into the room."
2852	""
2852	"      But there was no sign within it of that desperate and defiant"
2852	"      villain whom we expected to see. Instead we were faced by an"
2852	"      object so strange and so unexpected that we stood for a moment"
2852	"      staring at it in amazement."
2852	""
2852	"      The room had been fashioned into a small museum, and the walls"
2852	"      were lined by a number of glass-topped cases full of that"
2852	"      collection of butterflies and moths the formation of which had"
2852	"      been the relaxation of this complex and dangerous man. In the"
2852	"      centre of this room there was an upright beam, which had been"
2852	"      placed at some period as a support for the old worm-eaten baulk"
2852	"      of timber which spanned the roof. To this post a figure was tied,"
2852	"      so swathed and muffled in the sheets which had been used to"
2852	"      secure it that one could not for the moment tell whether it was"
2852	"      that of a man or a woman. One towel passed round the throat and"
2852	"      was secured at the back of the pillar. Another covered the lower"
2852	"      part of the face, and over it two dark eyes—eyes full of grief"
2852	"      and shame and a dreadful questioning—stared back at us. In a"
2852	"      minute we had torn off the gag, unswathed the bonds, and Mrs."
2852	"      Stapleton sank upon the floor in front of us. As her beautiful"
2852	"      head fell upon her chest I saw the clear red weal of a whiplash"
2852	"      across her neck."
2852	""
2852	"      “The brute!” cried Holmes. “Here, Lestrade, your brandy-bottle!"
2852	"      Put her in the chair! She has fainted from ill-usage and"
2852	"      exhaustion.”"
2852	""
2852	"      She opened her eyes again."
2852	""
2852	"      “Is he safe?” she asked. “Has he escaped?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “He cannot escape us, madam.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No, no, I did not mean my husband. Sir Henry? Is he safe?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Yes.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “And the hound?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “It is dead.”"
2852	""
2852	"      She gave a long sigh of satisfaction."
2852	""
2852	"      “Thank God! Thank God! Oh, this villain! See how he has treated"
2852	"      me!” She shot her arms out from her sleeves, and we saw with"
2852	"      horror that they were all mottled with bruises. “But this is"
2852	"      nothing—nothing! It is my mind and soul that he has tortured and"
2852	"      defiled. I could endure it all, ill-usage, solitude, a life of"
2852	"      deception, everything, as long as I could still cling to the hope"
2852	"      that I had his love, but now I know that in this also I have been"
2852	"      his dupe and his tool.” She broke into passionate sobbing as she"
2852	"      spoke."
2852	""
2852	"      “You bear him no good will, madam,” said Holmes. “Tell us then"
2852	"      where we shall find him. If you have ever aided him in evil, help"
2852	"      us now and so atone.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “There is but one place where he can have fled,” she answered."
2852	"      “There is an old tin mine on an island in the heart of the mire."
2852	"      It was there that he kept his hound and there also he had made"
2852	"      preparations so that he might have a refuge. That is where he"
2852	"      would fly.”"
2852	""
2852	"      The fog-bank lay like white wool against the window. Holmes held"
2852	"      the lamp towards it."
2852	""
2852	"      “See,” said he. “No one could find his way into the Grimpen Mire"
2852	"      tonight.”"
2852	""
2852	"      She laughed and clapped her hands. Her eyes and teeth gleamed"
2852	"      with fierce merriment."
2852	""
2852	"      “He may find his way in, but never out,” she cried. “How can he"
2852	"      see the guiding wands tonight? We planted them together, he and"
2852	"      I, to mark the pathway through the mire. Oh, if I could only have"
2852	"      plucked them out today. Then indeed you would have had him at"
2852	"      your mercy!”"
2852	""
2852	"      It was evident to us that all pursuit was in vain until the fog"
2852	"      had lifted. Meanwhile we left Lestrade in possession of the house"
2852	"      while Holmes and I went back with the baronet to Baskerville"
2852	"      Hall. The story of the Stapletons could no longer be withheld"
2852	"      from him, but he took the blow bravely when he learned the truth"
2852	"      about the woman whom he had loved. But the shock of the night’s"
2852	"      adventures had shattered his nerves, and before morning he lay"
2852	"      delirious in a high fever under the care of Dr. Mortimer. The two"
2852	"      of them were destined to travel together round the world before"
2852	"      Sir Henry had become once more the hale, hearty man that he had"
2852	"      been before he became master of that ill-omened estate."
2852	""
2852	"      And now I come rapidly to the conclusion of this singular"
2852	"      narrative, in which I have tried to make the reader share those"
2852	"      dark fears and vague surmises which clouded our lives so long and"
2852	"      ended in so tragic a manner. On the morning after the death of"
2852	"      the hound the fog had lifted and we were guided by Mrs. Stapleton"
2852	"      to the point where they had found a pathway through the bog. It"
2852	"      helped us to realise the horror of this woman’s life when we saw"
2852	"      the eagerness and joy with which she laid us on her husband’s"
2852	"      track. We left her standing upon the thin peninsula of firm,"
2852	"      peaty soil which tapered out into the widespread bog. From the"
2852	"      end of it a small wand planted here and there showed where the"
2852	"      path zigzagged from tuft to tuft of rushes among those"
2852	"      green-scummed pits and foul quagmires which barred the way to the"
2852	"      stranger. Rank reeds and lush, slimy water-plants sent an odour"
2852	"      of decay and a heavy miasmatic vapour onto our faces, while a"
2852	"      false step plunged us more than once thigh-deep into the dark,"
2852	"      quivering mire, which shook for yards in soft undulations around"
2852	"      our feet. Its tenacious grip plucked at our heels as we walked,"
2852	"      and when we sank into it it was as if some malignant hand was"
2852	"      tugging us down into those obscene depths, so grim and purposeful"
2852	"      was the clutch in which it held us. Once only we saw a trace that"
2852	"      someone had passed that perilous way before us. From amid a tuft"
2852	"      of cotton grass which bore it up out of the slime some dark thing"
2852	"      was projecting. Holmes sank to his waist as he stepped from the"
2852	"      path to seize it, and had we not been there to drag him out he"
2852	"      could never have set his foot upon firm land again. He held an"
2852	"      old black boot in the air. “Meyers, Toronto,” was printed on the"
2852	"      leather inside."
2852	""
2852	"      “It is worth a mud bath,” said he. “It is our friend Sir Henry’s"
2852	"      missing boot.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Thrown there by Stapleton in his flight.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Exactly. He retained it in his hand after using it to set the"
2852	"      hound upon the track. He fled when he knew the game was up, still"
2852	"      clutching it. And he hurled it away at this point of his flight."
2852	"      We know at least that he came so far in safety.”"
2852	""
2852	"      But more than that we were never destined to know, though there"
2852	"      was much which we might surmise. There was no chance of finding"
2852	"      footsteps in the mire, for the rising mud oozed swiftly in upon"
2852	"      them, but as we at last reached firmer ground beyond the morass"
2852	"      we all looked eagerly for them. But no slightest sign of them"
2852	"      ever met our eyes. If the earth told a true story, then Stapleton"
2852	"      never reached that island of refuge towards which he struggled"
2852	"      through the fog upon that last night. Somewhere in the heart of"
2852	"      the great Grimpen Mire, down in the foul slime of the huge morass"
2852	"      which had sucked him in, this cold and cruel-hearted man is"
2852	"      forever buried."
2852	""
2852	"      Many traces we found of him in the bog-girt island where he had"
2852	"      hid his savage ally. A huge driving-wheel and a shaft half-filled"
2852	"      with rubbish showed the position of an abandoned mine. Beside it"
2852	"      were the crumbling remains of the cottages of the miners, driven"
2852	"      away no doubt by the foul reek of the surrounding swamp. In one"
2852	"      of these a staple and chain with a quantity of gnawed bones"
2852	"      showed where the animal had been confined. A skeleton with a"
2852	"      tangle of brown hair adhering to it lay among the _débris_."
2852	""
2852	"      “A dog!” said Holmes. “By Jove, a curly-haired spaniel. Poor"
2852	"      Mortimer will never see his pet again. Well, I do not know that"
2852	"      this place contains any secret which we have not already"
2852	"      fathomed. He could hide his hound, but he could not hush its"
2852	"      voice, and hence came those cries which even in daylight were not"
2852	"      pleasant to hear. On an emergency he could keep the hound in the"
2852	"      out-house at Merripit, but it was always a risk, and it was only"
2852	"      on the supreme day, which he regarded as the end of all his"
2852	"      efforts, that he dared do it. This paste in the tin is no doubt"
2852	"      the luminous mixture with which the creature was daubed. It was"
2852	"      suggested, of course, by the story of the family hell-hound, and"
2852	"      by the desire to frighten old Sir Charles to death. No wonder the"
2852	"      poor devil of a convict ran and screamed, even as our friend did,"
2852	"      and as we ourselves might have done, when he saw such a creature"
2852	"      bounding through the darkness of the moor upon his track. It was"
2852	"      a cunning device, for, apart from the chance of driving your"
2852	"      victim to his death, what peasant would venture to inquire too"
2852	"      closely into such a creature should he get sight of it, as many"
2852	"      have done, upon the moor? I said it in London, Watson, and I say"
2852	"      it again now, that never yet have we helped to hunt down a more"
2852	"      dangerous man than he who is lying yonder”—he swept his long arm"
2852	"      towards the huge mottled expanse of green-splotched bog which"
2852	"      stretched away until it merged into the russet slopes of the"
2852	"      moor."
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"<Chapter 15>"
2852	"A Retrospection"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"      It was the end of November, and Holmes and I sat, upon a raw and"
2852	"      foggy night, on either side of a blazing fire in our sitting-room"
2852	"      in Baker Street. Since the tragic upshot of our visit to"
2852	"      Devonshire he had been engaged in two affairs of the utmost"
2852	"      importance, in the first of which he had exposed the atrocious"
2852	"      conduct of Colonel Upwood in connection with the famous card"
2852	"      scandal of the Nonpareil Club, while in the second he had"
2852	"      defended the unfortunate Mme. Montpensier from the charge of"
2852	"      murder which hung over her in connection with the death of her"
2852	"      step-daughter, Mlle. Carére, the young lady who, as it will be"
2852	"      remembered, was found six months later alive and married in New"
2852	"      York. My friend was in excellent spirits over the success which"
2852	"      had attended a succession of difficult and important cases, so"
2852	"      that I was able to induce him to discuss the details of the"
2852	"      Baskerville mystery. I had waited patiently for the opportunity"
2852	"      for I was aware that he would never permit cases to overlap, and"
2852	"      that his clear and logical mind would not be drawn from its"
2852	"      present work to dwell upon memories of the past. Sir Henry and"
2852	"      Dr. Mortimer were, however, in London, on their way to that long"
2852	"      voyage which had been recommended for the restoration of his"
2852	"      shattered nerves. They had called upon us that very afternoon, so"
2852	"      that it was natural that the subject should come up for"
2852	"      discussion."
2852	""
2852	"      “The whole course of events,” said Holmes, “from the point of"
2852	"      view of the man who called himself Stapleton was simple and"
2852	"      direct, although to us, who had no means in the beginning of"
2852	"      knowing the motives of his actions and could only learn part of"
2852	"      the facts, it all appeared exceedingly complex. I have had the"
2852	"      advantage of two conversations with Mrs. Stapleton, and the case"
2852	"      has now been so entirely cleared up that I am not aware that"
2852	"      there is anything which has remained a secret to us. You will"
2852	"      find a few notes upon the matter under the heading B in my"
2852	"      indexed list of cases.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Perhaps you would kindly give me a sketch of the course of"
2852	"      events from memory.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “Certainly, though I cannot guarantee that I carry all the facts"
2852	"      in my mind. Intense mental concentration has a curious way of"
2852	"      blotting out what has passed. The barrister who has his case at"
2852	"      his fingers’ ends and is able to argue with an expert upon his"
2852	"      own subject finds that a week or two of the courts will drive it"
2852	"      all out of his head once more. So each of my cases displaces the"
2852	"      last, and Mlle. Carére has blurred my recollection of Baskerville"
2852	"      Hall. Tomorrow some other little problem may be submitted to my"
2852	"      notice which will in turn dispossess the fair French lady and the"
2852	"      infamous Upwood. So far as the case of the hound goes, however, I"
2852	"      will give you the course of events as nearly as I can, and you"
2852	"      will suggest anything which I may have forgotten."
2852	""
2852	"      “My inquiries show beyond all question that the family portrait"
2852	"      did not lie, and that this fellow was indeed a Baskerville. He"
2852	"      was a son of that Rodger Baskerville, the younger brother of Sir"
2852	"      Charles, who fled with a sinister reputation to South America,"
2852	"      where he was said to have died unmarried. He did, as a matter of"
2852	"      fact, marry, and had one child, this fellow, whose real name is"
2852	"      the same as his father’s. He married Beryl Garcia, one of the"
2852	"      beauties of Costa Rica, and, having purloined a considerable sum"
2852	"      of public money, he changed his name to Vandeleur and fled to"
2852	"      England, where he established a school in the east of Yorkshire."
2852	"      His reason for attempting this special line of business was that"
2852	"      he had struck up an acquaintance with a consumptive tutor upon"
2852	"      the voyage home, and that he had used this man’s ability to make"
2852	"      the undertaking a success. Fraser, the tutor, died however, and"
2852	"      the school which had begun well sank from disrepute into infamy."
2852	"      The Vandeleurs found it convenient to change their name to"
2852	"      Stapleton, and he brought the remains of his fortune, his schemes"
2852	"      for the future, and his taste for entomology to the south of"
2852	"      England. I learned at the British Museum that he was a recognized"
2852	"      authority upon the subject, and that the name of Vandeleur has"
2852	"      been permanently attached to a certain moth which he had, in his"
2852	"      Yorkshire days, been the first to describe."
2852	""
2852	"      “We now come to that portion of his life which has proved to be"
2852	"      of such intense interest to us. The fellow had evidently made"
2852	"      inquiry and found that only two lives intervened between him and"
2852	"      a valuable estate. When he went to Devonshire his plans were, I"
2852	"      believe, exceedingly hazy, but that he meant mischief from the"
2852	"      first is evident from the way in which he took his wife with him"
2852	"      in the character of his sister. The idea of using her as a decoy"
2852	"      was clearly already in his mind, though he may not have been"
2852	"      certain how the details of his plot were to be arranged. He meant"
2852	"      in the end to have the estate, and he was ready to use any tool"
2852	"      or run any risk for that end. His first act was to establish"
2852	"      himself as near to his ancestral home as he could, and his second"
2852	"      was to cultivate a friendship with Sir Charles Baskerville and"
2852	"      with the neighbours."
2852	""
2852	"      “The baronet himself told him about the family hound, and so"
2852	"      prepared the way for his own death. Stapleton, as I will continue"
2852	"      to call him, knew that the old man’s heart was weak and that a"
2852	"      shock would kill him. So much he had learned from Dr. Mortimer."
2852	"      He had heard also that Sir Charles was superstitious and had"
2852	"      taken this grim legend very seriously. His ingenious mind"
2852	"      instantly suggested a way by which the baronet could be done to"
2852	"      death, and yet it would be hardly possible to bring home the"
2852	"      guilt to the real murderer."
2852	""
2852	"      “Having conceived the idea he proceeded to carry it out with"
2852	"      considerable finesse. An ordinary schemer would have been content"
2852	"      to work with a savage hound. The use of artificial means to make"
2852	"      the creature diabolical was a flash of genius upon his part. The"
2852	"      dog he bought in London from Ross and Mangles, the dealers in"
2852	"      Fulham Road. It was the strongest and most savage in their"
2852	"      possession. He brought it down by the North Devon line and walked"
2852	"      a great distance over the moor so as to get it home without"
2852	"      exciting any remarks. He had already on his insect hunts learned"
2852	"      to penetrate the Grimpen Mire, and so had found a safe"
2852	"      hiding-place for the creature. Here he kennelled it and waited"
2852	"      his chance."
2852	""
2852	"      “But it was some time coming. The old gentleman could not be"
2852	"      decoyed outside of his grounds at night. Several times Stapleton"
2852	"      lurked about with his hound, but without avail. It was during"
2852	"      these fruitless quests that he, or rather his ally, was seen by"
2852	"      peasants, and that the legend of the demon dog received a new"
2852	"      confirmation. He had hoped that his wife might lure Sir Charles"
2852	"      to his ruin, but here she proved unexpectedly independent. She"
2852	"      would not endeavour to entangle the old gentleman in a"
2852	"      sentimental attachment which might deliver him over to his enemy."
2852	"      Threats and even, I am sorry to say, blows refused to move her."
2852	"      She would have nothing to do with it, and for a time Stapleton"
2852	"      was at a deadlock."
2852	""
2852	"      “He found a way out of his difficulties through the chance that"
2852	"      Sir Charles, who had conceived a friendship for him, made him the"
2852	"      minister of his charity in the case of this unfortunate woman,"
2852	"      Mrs. Laura Lyons. By representing himself as a single man he"
2852	"      acquired complete influence over her, and he gave her to"
2852	"      understand that in the event of her obtaining a divorce from her"
2852	"      husband he would marry her. His plans were suddenly brought to a"
2852	"      head by his knowledge that Sir Charles was about to leave the"
2852	"      Hall on the advice of Dr. Mortimer, with whose opinion he himself"
2852	"      pretended to coincide. He must act at once, or his victim might"
2852	"      get beyond his power. He therefore put pressure upon Mrs. Lyons"
2852	"      to write this letter, imploring the old man to give her an"
2852	"      interview on the evening before his departure for London. He"
2852	"      then, by a specious argument, prevented her from going, and so"
2852	"      had the chance for which he had waited."
2852	""
2852	"      “Driving back in the evening from Coombe Tracey he was in time to"
2852	"      get his hound, to treat it with his infernal paint, and to bring"
2852	"      the beast round to the gate at which he had reason to expect that"
2852	"      he would find the old gentleman waiting. The dog, incited by its"
2852	"      master, sprang over the wicket-gate and pursued the unfortunate"
2852	"      baronet, who fled screaming down the yew alley. In that gloomy"
2852	"      tunnel it must indeed have been a dreadful sight to see that huge"
2852	"      black creature, with its flaming jaws and blazing eyes, bounding"
2852	"      after its victim. He fell dead at the end of the alley from heart"
2852	"      disease and terror. The hound had kept upon the grassy border"
2852	"      while the baronet had run down the path, so that no track but the"
2852	"      man’s was visible. On seeing him lying still the creature had"
2852	"      probably approached to sniff at him, but finding him dead had"
2852	"      turned away again. It was then that it left the print which was"
2852	"      actually observed by Dr. Mortimer. The hound was called off and"
2852	"      hurried away to its lair in the Grimpen Mire, and a mystery was"
2852	"      left which puzzled the authorities, alarmed the countryside, and"
2852	"      finally brought the case within the scope of our observation."
2852	""
2852	"      “So much for the death of Sir Charles Baskerville. You perceive"
2852	"      the devilish cunning of it, for really it would be almost"
2852	"      impossible to make a case against the real murderer. His only"
2852	"      accomplice was one who could never give him away, and the"
2852	"      grotesque, inconceivable nature of the device only served to make"
2852	"      it more effective. Both of the women concerned in the case, Mrs."
2852	"      Stapleton and Mrs. Laura Lyons, were left with a strong suspicion"
2852	"      against Stapleton. Mrs. Stapleton knew that he had designs upon"
2852	"      the old man, and also of the existence of the hound. Mrs. Lyons"
2852	"      knew neither of these things, but had been impressed by the death"
2852	"      occurring at the time of an uncancelled appointment which was"
2852	"      only known to him. However, both of them were under his"
2852	"      influence, and he had nothing to fear from them. The first half"
2852	"      of his task was successfully accomplished but the more difficult"
2852	"      still remained."
2852	""
2852	"      “It is possible that Stapleton did not know of the existence of"
2852	"      an heir in Canada. In any case he would very soon learn it from"
2852	"      his friend Dr. Mortimer, and he was told by the latter all"
2852	"      details about the arrival of Henry Baskerville. Stapleton’s first"
2852	"      idea was that this young stranger from Canada might possibly be"
2852	"      done to death in London without coming down to Devonshire at all."
2852	"      He distrusted his wife ever since she had refused to help him in"
2852	"      laying a trap for the old man, and he dared not leave her long"
2852	"      out of his sight for fear he should lose his influence over her."
2852	"      It was for this reason that he took her to London with him. They"
2852	"      lodged, I find, at the Mexborough Private Hotel, in Craven"
2852	"      Street, which was actually one of those called upon by my agent"
2852	"      in search of evidence. Here he kept his wife imprisoned in her"
2852	"      room while he, disguised in a beard, followed Dr. Mortimer to"
2852	"      Baker Street and afterwards to the station and to the"
2852	"      Northumberland Hotel. His wife had some inkling of his plans; but"
2852	"      she had such a fear of her husband—a fear founded upon brutal"
2852	"      ill-treatment—that she dare not write to warn the man whom she"
2852	"      knew to be in danger. If the letter should fall into Stapleton’s"
2852	"      hands her own life would not be safe. Eventually, as we know, she"
2852	"      adopted the expedient of cutting out the words which would form"
2852	"      the message, and addressing the letter in a disguised hand. It"
2852	"      reached the baronet, and gave him the first warning of his"
2852	"      danger."
2852	""
2852	"      “It was very essential for Stapleton to get some article of Sir"
2852	"      Henry’s attire so that, in case he was driven to use the dog, he"
2852	"      might always have the means of setting him upon his track. With"
2852	"      characteristic promptness and audacity he set about this at once,"
2852	"      and we cannot doubt that the boots or chamber-maid of the hotel"
2852	"      was well bribed to help him in his design. By chance, however,"
2852	"      the first boot which was procured for him was a new one and,"
2852	"      therefore, useless for his purpose. He then had it returned and"
2852	"      obtained another—a most instructive incident, since it proved"
2852	"      conclusively to my mind that we were dealing with a real hound,"
2852	"      as no other supposition could explain this anxiety to obtain an"
2852	"      old boot and this indifference to a new one. The more _outré_ and"
2852	"      grotesque an incident is the more carefully it deserves to be"
2852	"      examined, and the very point which appears to complicate a case"
2852	"      is, when duly considered and scientifically handled, the one"
2852	"      which is most likely to elucidate it."
2852	""
2852	"      “Then we had the visit from our friends next morning, shadowed"
2852	"      always by Stapleton in the cab. From his knowledge of our rooms"
2852	"      and of my appearance, as well as from his general conduct, I am"
2852	"      inclined to think that Stapleton’s career of crime has been by no"
2852	"      means limited to this single Baskerville affair. It is suggestive"
2852	"      that during the last three years there have been four"
2852	"      considerable burglaries in the west country, for none of which"
2852	"      was any criminal ever arrested. The last of these, at Folkestone"
2852	"      Court, in May, was remarkable for the cold-blooded pistolling of"
2852	"      the page, who surprised the masked and solitary burglar. I cannot"
2852	"      doubt that Stapleton recruited his waning resources in this"
2852	"      fashion, and that for years he has been a desperate and dangerous"
2852	"      man."
2852	""
2852	"      “We had an example of his readiness of resource that morning when"
2852	"      he got away from us so successfully, and also of his audacity in"
2852	"      sending back my own name to me through the cabman. From that"
2852	"      moment he understood that I had taken over the case in London,"
2852	"      and that therefore there was no chance for him there. He returned"
2852	"      to Dartmoor and awaited the arrival of the baronet.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “One moment!” said I. “You have, no doubt, described the sequence"
2852	"      of events correctly, but there is one point which you have left"
2852	"      unexplained. What became of the hound when its master was in"
2852	"      London?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “I have given some attention to this matter and it is undoubtedly"
2852	"      of importance. There can be no question that Stapleton had a"
2852	"      confidant, though it is unlikely that he ever placed himself in"
2852	"      his power by sharing all his plans with him. There was an old"
2852	"      manservant at Merripit House, whose name was Anthony. His"
2852	"      connection with the Stapletons can be traced for several years,"
2852	"      as far back as the school-mastering days, so that he must have"
2852	"      been aware that his master and mistress were really husband and"
2852	"      wife. This man has disappeared and has escaped from the country."
2852	"      It is suggestive that Anthony is not a common name in England,"
2852	"      while Antonio is so in all Spanish or Spanish-American countries."
2852	"      The man, like Mrs. Stapleton herself, spoke good English, but"
2852	"      with a curious lisping accent. I have myself seen this old man"
2852	"      cross the Grimpen Mire by the path which Stapleton had marked"
2852	"      out. It is very probable, therefore, that in the absence of his"
2852	"      master it was he who cared for the hound, though he may never"
2852	"      have known the purpose for which the beast was used."
2852	""
2852	"      “The Stapletons then went down to Devonshire, whither they were"
2852	"      soon followed by Sir Henry and you. One word now as to how I"
2852	"      stood myself at that time. It may possibly recur to your memory"
2852	"      that when I examined the paper upon which the printed words were"
2852	"      fastened I made a close inspection for the water-mark. In doing"
2852	"      so I held it within a few inches of my eyes, and was conscious of"
2852	"      a faint smell of the scent known as white jessamine. There are"
2852	"      seventy-five perfumes, which it is very necessary that a criminal"
2852	"      expert should be able to distinguish from each other, and cases"
2852	"      have more than once within my own experience depended upon their"
2852	"      prompt recognition. The scent suggested the presence of a lady,"
2852	"      and already my thoughts began to turn towards the Stapletons."
2852	"      Thus I had made certain of the hound, and had guessed at the"
2852	"      criminal before ever we went to the west country."
2852	""
2852	"      “It was my game to watch Stapleton. It was evident, however, that"
2852	"      I could not do this if I were with you, since he would be keenly"
2852	"      on his guard. I deceived everybody, therefore, yourself included,"
2852	"      and I came down secretly when I was supposed to be in London. My"
2852	"      hardships were not so great as you imagined, though such trifling"
2852	"      details must never interfere with the investigation of a case. I"
2852	"      stayed for the most part at Coombe Tracey, and only used the hut"
2852	"      upon the moor when it was necessary to be near the scene of"
2852	"      action. Cartwright had come down with me, and in his disguise as"
2852	"      a country boy he was of great assistance to me. I was dependent"
2852	"      upon him for food and clean linen. When I was watching Stapleton,"
2852	"      Cartwright was frequently watching you, so that I was able to"
2852	"      keep my hand upon all the strings."
2852	""
2852	"      “I have already told you that your reports reached me rapidly,"
2852	"      being forwarded instantly from Baker Street to Coombe Tracey."
2852	"      They were of great service to me, and especially that one"
2852	"      incidentally truthful piece of biography of Stapleton’s. I was"
2852	"      able to establish the identity of the man and the woman and knew"
2852	"      at last exactly how I stood. The case had been considerably"
2852	"      complicated through the incident of the escaped convict and the"
2852	"      relations between him and the Barrymores. This also you cleared"
2852	"      up in a very effective way, though I had already come to the same"
2852	"      conclusions from my own observations."
2852	""
2852	"      “By the time that you discovered me upon the moor I had a"
2852	"      complete knowledge of the whole business, but I had not a case"
2852	"      which could go to a jury. Even Stapleton’s attempt upon Sir Henry"
2852	"      that night which ended in the death of the unfortunate convict"
2852	"      did not help us much in proving murder against our man. There"
2852	"      seemed to be no alternative but to catch him red-handed, and to"
2852	"      do so we had to use Sir Henry, alone and apparently unprotected,"
2852	"      as a bait. We did so, and at the cost of a severe shock to our"
2852	"      client we succeeded in completing our case and driving Stapleton"
2852	"      to his destruction. That Sir Henry should have been exposed to"
2852	"      this is, I must confess, a reproach to my management of the case,"
2852	"      but we had no means of foreseeing the terrible and paralyzing"
2852	"      spectacle which the beast presented, nor could we predict the fog"
2852	"      which enabled him to burst upon us at such short notice. We"
2852	"      succeeded in our object at a cost which both the specialist and"
2852	"      Dr. Mortimer assure me will be a temporary one. A long journey"
2852	"      may enable our friend to recover not only from his shattered"
2852	"      nerves but also from his wounded feelings. His love for the lady"
2852	"      was deep and sincere, and to him the saddest part of all this"
2852	"      black business was that he should have been deceived by her."
2852	""
2852	"      “It only remains to indicate the part which she had played"
2852	"      throughout. There can be no doubt that Stapleton exercised an"
2852	"      influence over her which may have been love or may have been"
2852	"      fear, or very possibly both, since they are by no means"
2852	"      incompatible emotions. It was, at least, absolutely effective. At"
2852	"      his command she consented to pass as his sister, though he found"
2852	"      the limits of his power over her when he endeavoured to make her"
2852	"      the direct accessory to murder. She was ready to warn Sir Henry"
2852	"      so far as she could without implicating her husband, and again"
2852	"      and again she tried to do so. Stapleton himself seems to have"
2852	"      been capable of jealousy, and when he saw the baronet paying"
2852	"      court to the lady, even though it was part of his own plan, still"
2852	"      he could not help interrupting with a passionate outburst which"
2852	"      revealed the fiery soul which his self-contained manner so"
2852	"      cleverly concealed. By encouraging the intimacy he made it"
2852	"      certain that Sir Henry would frequently come to Merripit House"
2852	"      and that he would sooner or later get the opportunity which he"
2852	"      desired. On the day of the crisis, however, his wife turned"
2852	"      suddenly against him. She had learned something of the death of"
2852	"      the convict, and she knew that the hound was being kept in the"
2852	"      outhouse on the evening that Sir Henry was coming to dinner. She"
2852	"      taxed her husband with his intended crime, and a furious scene"
2852	"      followed in which he showed her for the first time that she had a"
2852	"      rival in his love. Her fidelity turned in an instant to bitter"
2852	"      hatred, and he saw that she would betray him. He tied her up,"
2852	"      therefore, that she might have no chance of warning Sir Henry,"
2852	"      and he hoped, no doubt, that when the whole countryside put down"
2852	"      the baronet’s death to the curse of his family, as they certainly"
2852	"      would do, he could win his wife back to accept an accomplished"
2852	"      fact and to keep silent upon what she knew. In this I fancy that"
2852	"      in any case he made a miscalculation, and that, if we had not"
2852	"      been there, his doom would none the less have been sealed. A"
2852	"      woman of Spanish blood does not condone such an injury so"
2852	"      lightly. And now, my dear Watson, without referring to my notes,"
2852	"      I cannot give you a more detailed account of this curious case. I"
2852	"      do not know that anything essential has been left unexplained.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “He could not hope to frighten Sir Henry to death as he had done"
2852	"      the old uncle with his bogie hound.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “The beast was savage and half-starved. If its appearance did not"
2852	"      frighten its victim to death, at least it would paralyze the"
2852	"      resistance which might be offered.”"
2852	""
2852	"      “No doubt. There only remains one difficulty. If Stapleton came"
2852	"      into the succession, how could he explain the fact that he, the"
2852	"      heir, had been living unannounced under another name so close to"
2852	"      the property? How could he claim it without causing suspicion and"
2852	"      inquiry?”"
2852	""
2852	"      “It is a formidable difficulty, and I fear that you ask too much"
2852	"      when you expect me to solve it. The past and the present are"
2852	"      within the field of my inquiry, but what a man may do in the"
2852	"      future is a hard question to answer. Mrs. Stapleton has heard her"
2852	"      husband discuss the problem on several occasions. There were"
2852	"      three possible courses. He might claim the property from South"
2852	"      America, establish his identity before the British authorities"
2852	"      there and so obtain the fortune without ever coming to England at"
2852	"      all, or he might adopt an elaborate disguise during the short"
2852	"      time that he need be in London; or, again, he might furnish an"
2852	"      accomplice with the proofs and papers, putting him in as heir,"
2852	"      and retaining a claim upon some proportion of his income. We"
2852	"      cannot doubt from what we know of him that he would have found"
2852	"      some way out of the difficulty. And now, my dear Watson, we have"
2852	"      had some weeks of severe work, and for one evening, I think, we"
2852	"      may turn our thoughts into more pleasant channels. I have a box"
2852	"      for _Les Huguenots_. Have you heard the De Reszkes? Might I"
2852	"      trouble you then to be ready in half an hour, and we can stop at"
2852	"      Marcini’s for a little dinner on the way?”"
2852	""
2852	""
2852	"THE END"
